Al-Razi's Two Senses of Power
Al-Razi distinguishes two senses of power and their implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's distinction between power as a generic origin of acts and power as fully conditioned efficacy, and its implications for time and opposites.

Core Idea
Power as fully conditioned is simultaneous with the act and excludes opposites.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi offers a nuanced analysis of the concept of power (qudra), a key attribute in kalam. He shows that how we define power affects whether it can exist before an act or relate to opposite acts. This passage also connects the distinction to the Ash'ari doctrine of acquisition (kasb). Understanding these nuances clarifies debates about divine and human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp al-Razi's full argument.
Notice the two 'إن أريد' conditionals that structure the passage.
2
Identify the two senses of power
Find where al-Razi presents the first sense (power as mere origin) and the second sense (power with perfected conditions). Note the different implications for temporality and opposites.
The first sense allows power before, with, and after the act; the second sense only with the act.
3
Connect to Ash'ari kasb
See how al-Razi links the second sense to the Ash'ari position that originated power does not create (ijad) but is reinterpreted as kasb through habitual conditions.
This resolves a potential conflict between the two senses and Ash'ari theology.
4
Summarize the result
Al-Razi concludes that power with all its aspects is concomitant with the act necessarily or by custom; without those conditions it is prior. Grasp this final point.
This conclusion harmonizes the different senses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأورد الإمام الرازي كلاماً حاصله : إنه إن أريد بالقدرة القوة التي هي مبدأ الأفعال المختلفة سواء كملت جهات تأثيرها أو لم تكمل، فلا شك في كونها قبل الفعل ومعه وبعده. وفي جواز تعلقها بالضدين. وإن أريد القوة التي كملت جهات تأثيرها فلا خفاء في كونها مع الفعل بالزمان لا قبله وفي امتناع تعلقها بالضدين. بل بأن المقدورين مطلقاً ضرورة أن الشرائط المخصصة لهذا غير الشرائط المخصصة لذاك إلا أن الشيخ لما لم يقل بتأثير القدرة الحادثة. (بمعنى الإيجاد فسرنا التأثير والمبدائية بما يعم الكسب الذي هو شأن القوة الحادثة). وذلك بحصول جميع الشرائط التي جرت العادة بحصول الفعل عندها فصار الحاصل أن القوة مع جميع جهات حصول الفعل بها لزوماً أو معها عادة مقارنة وبدون ذلك سابقة.
And Imam al-Razi presented a discourse the gist of which: that if by 'power' is meant the force that is the origin [mabda'] of different acts, whether its aspects of efficacy are perfected or not, then there is no doubt that it exists before the act, with it, and after it, and in the permissibility of its relation to opposites. But if by 'power' is meant the force whose aspects of efficacy are perfected, then it is no secret that it exists with the act in time, not before it, and in the impossibility of its relation to opposites. Rather, with respect to the two objects of power absolutely, because the conditions specific to one are not the conditions specific to the other—except that the Shaykh (al-Ash'ari) since he did not affirm the effect of the originated power (in the sense of creation), we interpreted 'effect' and 'origination' to include acquisition (kasb), which is the concern of the originated power. And that is by the attainment of all the conditions at which the act habitually occurs. So the result is that the force with all its aspects is concomitant with the occurrence of the act by it necessarily or habitually, and without that it is prior.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage presents Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's analysis of the concept of power (qudra). He distinguishes two senses: (1) power as a generic force (quwwa) that is the origin of various acts, regardless of whether its conditions of efficacy are fully realized. In this sense, power exists before, during, and after the act, and it can relate to opposites (i.e., the agent can do either). (2) Power as a force whose conditions of efficacy are fully perfected. In this sense, power is simultaneous with the act in time, not prior, and cannot relate to opposites because the specific conditions for one act differ from those for the other. Al-Razi then connects this to the Ash'ari doctrine of acquisition (kasb): since al-Ash'ari (the Shaykh) did not affirm that the originated power creates (ijad) the act, the terms 'effect' and 'origination' (ta'thir wa mabda'iyya) are reinterpreted to include kasb. The act occurs by the attainment of all conditions that customarily bring it about. Thus, the power with all its aspects is concomitant with the act either necessarily or by custom, and without those conditions it is prior (sabiqa).

Key Terms
القوة
مبدأ الأفعال
جهات التأثير
الضدان
الكسب
القوة الحادثة
الشيخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Razi denies power can exist before the act altogether.
Correction: Al-Razi only denies prior existence for power in the fully conditioned sense; the generic sense allows power before the act.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, in which sense can power relate to opposites?
A) When power is meant as the mere origin of different acts, without full conditions.
B) When power is meant as the force with fully perfected conditions.
C) In neither sense.
D) In both senses.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's two-sense distinction shows that power as fully conditioned is simultaneous with the act and cannot relate to opposites, which aligns with the Ash'ari reinterpretation of kasb.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the conditions around your own actions—your health, opportunity, resources—do you ever feel that your power is truly 'fully conditioned'? What does that feeling reveal about your reliance on Allah versus your own ability?
?
Al-Razi says that power in the fully conditioned sense is simultaneous with the act, not before it. Does that make you uncomfortable? How does it challenge your sense of being the 'origin' of your choices? Will you still take responsibility for your actions knowing they are really acquired from Allah?
?
If your power to act depends on conditions that are ultimately given by Allah, what does that mean for your moments of pride or shame? When you succeed, are you really the one who made it happen? When you fail, is your power truly absent, or is it a reminder that all conditions are from Allah?

Classification of Power: Heart-Power and Limb-Power
Author presents three classifications of heart and limb power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three different views on how power residing in the heart versus the limbs relates to actions, as presented by the author.

Core Idea
Power is classified by its locus and scope of action.

This passage introduces a debate about how power (qudra) that resides in the heart versus the limbs relates to various actions. The author presents three different opinions on this matter, which are foundational for understanding the Ash'ari theory of acquisition (kasb).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the first part of the Arabic passage without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through.
The passage uses technical terms like القائمة بالقلب and القائمة بالجوارح.
2
Identify the three views
The passage presents three views on the relation of heart-power and limb-power to acts. Note the key differences: first view says heart-power can relate to both but cannot unite with limb acts; second view says each power is restricted to its own locus; third view says heart-power relates to both even if it doesn't affect limbs.
The author indicates that the last two views are referred to in the matan.
3
Understand the author's comment
The author explains that the matan alluded to the last two views by specifying 'the two judgments' as relating to the heart only, or as relating to all acts of its locus.
This shows how the text has compressed these ideas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائمة بالقلب تتعلق بأفعال القلوب والجوارح لكن يمتنع اتحاد أفعال الجوارح بها لفقد الشرائط والقائمة بالجوارح بالعكس. وتارة أن القائمة بالقلب تتعلق بجميع أفعال القلب، والقائمة بالجوارح أفعال الجوارح. لا تتعلق بجميع وتارة أن القائمة بالقلب تتعلق بأفعال القلوب والجوارح جميعاً وإن لم تؤثر في أفعال الجوارح، والقائمة بالجوارح لا تتعلق بأفعال القلب، وإلى القولين الأخيرين أشار في المتن بقوله وتارة خص الحكمين بالقلبية، وأراد بالحكمين أفعال محله خاصة والتعلق بجميع أفعال محله، ومحل الأخرى التعلق بجميع وأورد الإمام الرازي كلاماً حاصله : إنه إن أريد بالقدرة القوة التي هي مبدأ الأفعال المختلفة سواء كملت جهات تأثيرها أو لم تكمل، فلا شك في كونها قبل الفعل ومعه وبعده. وفي جواز تعلقها بالضدين. وإن أريد القوة التي جهات تأثيرها فلا خفاء في كونها مع الفعل بالزمان لا قبله وفي امتناع تعلقها بالضدين [؟] بال بالمقدورين مطلقاً ضرورة أن الشرائط المخصصة لهذا غير الشرائط المخصصة لذاك إلا أن الشيخ لما لم يقل بتأثير القدرة الحادثة (بمعنى الإيجاد فسرنا التأثير والمبدائية بما يعم الكسب الذي هو شأن القوة الحادثة). وذلك بحصول جميع الشرائط التي جرت العادة بحصول الفعل عندها فصار الحاصل أن القوة مع جميع جهات حصول الفعل بها لزوماً أو معها عادة مقارنة وبدون ذلك سابقة.
The power residing in the heart pertains to acts of the heart and limbs, but the union of the acts of the limbs with it is impossible due to the absence of conditions, and the power residing in the limbs is the opposite. Sometimes the power residing in the heart pertains to all acts of the heart, and the power residing in the limbs pertains to acts of the limbs. It does not pertain to all. And sometimes the power residing in the heart pertains to acts of the heart and limbs all together even if it does not affect the acts of the limbs, and the power residing in the limbs does not pertain to acts of the heart. And the author indicated the last two opinions in the text by saying: 'And sometimes he specified the two judgments to the heart-related,' meaning by 'the two judgments' the acts of its locus specifically and the relation to all acts of its locus, and the locus of the other [power] is the relation to all. And Imam al-Razi reported a statement the summary of which is: If by 'power' is meant the force that is the source of different acts, whether its aspects of efficacy are complete or not, then there is no doubt that it is before the act, with it, and after it, and that it is permissible for it to be related to both opposites. And if by 'power' is meant the force whose aspects of efficacy [are complete], then it is obvious that it is with the act temporally, not before it, and that it is impossible for it to be related to both opposites [?] by [something] regarding the two empowerments absolutely, necessarily because the conditions specific to this are other than the conditions specific to that; except that the shaykh, since he did not affirm the effect of the originated power (in the sense of bringing into existence), we interpreted 'effect' and 'origination' to include acquisition (kasb) which is the property of the originated power. And that is by the occurrence of all the conditions whose custom is that the act occurs with them; so the result is that the force with all the aspects of the act's occurrence by it, necessarily or customarily, is concomitant, and without that it is prior.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about the classification of power (qudra) as it relates to actions, followed by a clarification from Imam al-Razi. The author first presents different views on how power residing in the heart (al-qa'imah bil-qalb) and power residing in the limbs (al-qa'imah bil-jawarih) relate to actions. One view says heart-power pertains to heart and limb acts but cannot unite with limb acts due to missing conditions; limb-power is the opposite. Another view says heart-power pertains only to heart acts, limb-power only to limb acts. A third view says heart-power pertains to both heart and limb acts even if it doesn't affect limbs, while limb-power does not pertain to heart acts. The author then says the text (al-matan) alluded to the last two opinions. Then al-Razi's statement is quoted: if 'power' means the underlying force that is the source of various acts, regardless of whether its aspects of efficacy are complete, then it exists before, during, and after the act, and can relate to both opposites (e.g., performing or omitting). But if 'power' means the force whose aspects of efficacy are complete (i.e., all conditions are present), then it exists only with the act, not before, and cannot relate to both opposites. The author then comments that since al-Ash'ari (the shaykh) did not affirm that originated power actually creates (ijad), the terms 'effect' and 'origination' are interpreted to include acquisition (kasb), which is the property of the originated power. The complete set of customary conditions produces the act, so the force with all conditions is concomitant (with the act), and without them it is prior (to the act).

Key Terms
القائمة بالقلب
القائمة بالجوارح
القدرة
الشرائط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that heart-power cannot affect limb acts at all.
Correction: The first opinion says it can, but union is impossible due to missing conditions, not because it is inherently incapable.

Checkpoint
According to the first view presented, what is the relationship between heart-power and limb acts?
A) It pertains to limb acts and unites with them
B) It pertains to limb acts but cannot unite due to missing conditions
C) It does not pertain to limb acts at all
D) It pertains only to heart acts

Key Takeaway
The classification of power by its locus (heart vs. limbs) determines the scope of actions each can potentially affect, with three distinct opinions recorded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt a strong intention in your heart to do a good deed, but your limbs failed to act? According to the first opinion, this limitation is not due to a deficiency in your heart's power but rather missing conditions. What conditions might you be lacking? Ask yourself honestly: Is it a lack of sincerity, knowledge, or discipline? Commit to identifying one condition you can work on today.
?
The second opinion restricts each power to its own domain. In your life, do you sometimes expect your heart's intentions to automatically result in actions? How has that assumption led to spiritual neglect? What is one way you can better connect your inner resolve to outward obedience?
?
The third opinion acknowledges that heart-power pertains to all acts even if it does not affect limb acts. This suggests that your inner state matters intrinsically, not just for what it produces. When was the last time you judged your prayer or charity solely by its outward effect? How does this lesson challenge you to value your inner sincerity even when you see no visible result?

Al-Razi's Clarification on Power and Kasb
Al-Razi clarifies the two meanings of power and the author relates this to kasb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's distinction between two meanings of power (general force vs. complete efficacy) and how this relates to the Ash'ari doctrine of acquisition (kasb).

Core Idea
Power has two meanings: one that exists before the act and one that accompanies it.

Imam al-Razi offers a crucial clarification on the concept of power (qudra), distinguishing between the underlying force that can precede an act and the complete efficacy that is simultaneous with it. This distinction helps resolve debates about whether power can relate to opposites and how it connects to the Ash'ari concept of acquisition (kasb).

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's statement
Read the Arabic passage from 'وأورد الإمام الرازي' to the end. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'If by power is meant... then... and if by power is meant... then...'
Al-Razi's statement is a summary (حاصله) of a longer argument.
2
Identify the two meanings of power
The first meaning is the general force that is the source of different acts, regardless of whether its aspects of efficacy are complete. The second meaning is the force whose aspects of efficacy are complete.
The first meaning allows power to exist before, with, and after the act, and to relate to both opposites. The second meaning restricts power to existing only with the act and cannot relate to both opposites.
3
Understand the condition about opposites
Al-Razi says that for the second meaning, it is impossible for power to relate to both opposites because the conditions specific to each opposite are different.
This is a key point in the debate about human agency and divine omnipotence.
4
Connect to the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb
The author explains that since al-Ash'ari (the shaykh) did not affirm that the originated power actually creates (ijad), the terms 'effect' and 'origination' are reinterpreted to include acquisition (kasb). The complete set of customary conditions produces the act, so the force with all conditions is concomitant with the act, and without them it is prior.
This shows how the Ash'ari school reconciles the two meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائمة بالقلب تتعلق بأفعال القلوب والجوارح لكن يمتنع اتحاد أفعال الجوارح بها لفقد الشرائط والقائمة بالجوارح بالعكس. وتارة أن القائمة بالقلب تتعلق بجميع أفعال القلب، والقائمة بالجوارح أفعال الجوارح. لا تتعلق بجميع وتارة أن القائمة بالقلب تتعلق بأفعال القلوب والجوارح جميعاً وإن لم تؤثر في أفعال الجوارح، والقائمة بالجوارح لا تتعلق بأفعال القلب، وإلى القولين الأخيرين أشار في المتن بقوله وتارة خص الحكمين بالقلبية، وأراد بالحكمين أفعال محله خاصة والتعلق بجميع أفعال محله، ومحل الأخرى التعلق بجميع وأورد الإمام الرازي كلاماً حاصله : إنه إن أريد بالقدرة القوة التي هي مبدأ الأفعال المختلفة سواء كملت جهات تأثيرها أو لم تكمل، فلا شك في كونها قبل الفعل ومعه وبعده. وفي جواز تعلقها بالضدين. وإن أريد القوة التي جهات تأثيرها فلا خفاء في كونها مع الفعل بالزمان لا قبله وفي امتناع تعلقها بالضدين [؟] بال بالمقدورين مطلقاً ضرورة أن الشرائط المخصصة لهذا غير الشرائط المخصصة لذاك إلا أن الشيخ لما لم يقل بتأثير القدرة الحادثة (بمعنى الإيجاد فسرنا التأثير والمبدائية بما يعم الكسب الذي هو شأن القوة الحادثة). وذلك بحصول جميع الشرائط التي جرت العادة بحصول الفعل عندها فصار الحاصل أن القوة مع جميع جهات حصول الفعل بها لزوماً أو معها عادة مقارنة وبدون ذلك سابقة.
The power residing in the heart pertains to acts of the heart and limbs, but the union of the acts of the limbs with it is impossible due to the absence of conditions, and the power residing in the limbs is the opposite. Sometimes the power residing in the heart pertains to all acts of the heart, and the power residing in the limbs pertains to acts of the limbs. It does not pertain to all. And sometimes the power residing in the heart pertains to acts of the heart and limbs all together even if it does not affect the acts of the limbs, and the power residing in the limbs does not pertain to acts of the heart. And the author indicated the last two opinions in the text by saying: 'And sometimes he specified the two judgments to the heart-related,' meaning by 'the two judgments' the acts of its locus specifically and the relation to all acts of its locus, and the locus of the other [power] is the relation to all. And Imam al-Razi reported a statement the summary of which is: If by 'power' is meant the force that is the source of different acts, whether its aspects of efficacy are complete or not, then there is no doubt that it is before the act, with it, and after it, and that it is permissible for it to be related to both opposites. And if by 'power' is meant the force whose aspects of efficacy [are complete], then it is obvious that it is with the act temporally, not before it, and that it is impossible for it to be related to both opposites [?] by [something] regarding the two empowerments absolutely, necessarily because the conditions specific to this are other than the conditions specific to that; except that the shaykh, since he did not affirm the effect of the originated power (in the sense of bringing into existence), we interpreted 'effect' and 'origination' to include acquisition (kasb) which is the property of the originated power. And that is by the occurrence of all the conditions whose custom is that the act occurs with them; so the result is that the force with all the aspects of the act's occurrence by it, necessarily or customarily, is concomitant, and without that it is prior.

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about the classification of power (qudra) as it relates to actions, followed by a clarification from Imam al-Razi. The author first presents different views on how power residing in the heart (al-qa'imah bil-qalb) and power residing in the limbs (al-qa'imah bil-jawarih) relate to actions. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that power is only present at the moment of action and cannot precede it.
Correction: Al-Razi clarifies that if we mean the general force, power can exist before the act. If we mean complete efficacy, it is only with the act.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, when does power (in the sense of the force with complete aspects of efficacy) exist?
A) Before the act, with the act, and after it
B) Only with the act temporally
C) Only before the act
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's distinction between power as a general force and power as complete efficacy resolves debates about power's temporal relation to acts and its ability to relate to opposites, and the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb reframes 'effect' to mean acquisition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you attempt to do something difficult, do you rely on your own sense of ability or do you consciously acknowledge that your power is only a force that Allah creates and completes? How would this lesson change your daily reliance on Him?
?
Think of a time you failed despite having the ability. Did you assume your power was enough without the proper conditions? How does al-Razi's distinction challenge your understanding of why things succeed or fail?
?
The Ash'ari doctrine of kasb teaches that your action is acquired, not created by you. Does this make you feel more or less responsible for your deeds? How should it affect the way you seek forgiveness and strive to obey?

intermediate
6 min
Incapacity as the Opposite of Power
The author defines incapacity as the opposite of power and refutes Abu Hashim's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why incapacity is a real opposite attribute to power and not merely the absence of power, based on the distinction between the chronically ill and the externally prevented.

Core Idea
Incapacity is a real attribute opposite to power, not just its absence.

In this passage, the author establishes that incapacity (al-‘ajz) is a true opposite (ḍidd) of power, contrary to the Mu'tazilite view of Abu Hashim who considered it merely the absence of the property (malaka). The key evidence comes from comparing a chronically ill person (zamin) who lacks the ability to act due to an inherent condition, versus a person externally prevented (mamnu‘) who could have acted otherwise. Both lack power, but only the ill person has a real attribute of incapacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two positions
Note that the author states the majority view (incapacity as opposite) and Abu Hashim's view (absence of malaka). Look for the evidence given for each.
3
Understand the key evidence
The author uses the difference between a chronically ill person (zamin) and a prevented person (mamnu‘). Both lack power, but only the zamin has an inherent incapacity. This shows incapacity is more than just absence of power.
Think: Why can't a prevented person be called 'incapable' in the same way?
4
Examine the consequence and refutation
The author mentions a view attributed to al-Ash'ari that the object of incapacity is an existent thing, then refutes it using the example of the Qur'anic challenge.
This part is subtle; the commentary clarifies it.
5
Conclude with the majority view
The passage ends with the majority affirming incapacity as a fixed accident opposite to power. Compare this to Abu Hashim's view and note the reasoning.
This solidifies the lesson's main point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث : العجز ضد القدرة لا عدم ملكة كما هو رأي أبي هاشم لما نجد من ذلك الفرق بين الزمن والممنوع مع اشتراكهما في عدم القدرة، وله أن يمنع في الممنوع ، أو يجعل الفرق أن من شأنه القدرة بخلاف الزمن . ويتفرع عن التضاد ما نقل عن الشيخ، وإن كان خلاف الظاهر أن متعلق العجز هو الموجود حتى إن الزمن عاجز عن القعود بمعنى أن فيه صفة تستعقب القعود لا عن قدرة، ويبطله القطع بأن عجز المتحدين إنما هو عن الإتيان بمثل القرآن والتزام اشتراك اللفظ بين تلك الصفة، وعدم القدرة خلاف اللغة . الجمهور على أن العجز عرض ثابت مضاد للقدرة للقطع بأن في الزمن معنى لا يوجد في الممنوع مع اشتراكهما في عدم التمكن من الفعل . وعند أبي هاشم هو عدم ملكة للقدرة ، وليس في الزمن صفة متحققة تضاد
The third section: Incapacity is the opposite of power, not the absence of the property (malaka) as is the view of Abu Hashim, because we find from that the difference between the chronically ill (zamin) and the one prevented (mamnu‘) despite their sharing in the lack of power. And he [Abu Hashim] can deny [the existence of incapacity] in the one prevented, or make the difference that the one prevented has the potential for power unlike the chronically ill. And from the opposition [between incapacity and power] follows what is transmitted from the shaykh, although it is contrary to the apparent meaning: that the object of incapacity is the existent, such that the chronically ill is incapable of sitting, meaning that there is in him an attribute that entails sitting not due to power, and this is invalidated by the certainty that the incapacity of those who challenge is only about bringing the like of the Qur'an, and the necessary homonymy of the word between that attribute and the lack of power is contrary to language. The majority hold that incapacity is a fixed accident (‘arḍ) opposite to power, due to the certainty that in the chronically ill there is a meaning not found in the one prevented, despite their sharing in the lack of ability to act. According to Abu Hashim, it is the absence of the property of power, and there is in the chronically ill no real attribute that is opposite.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage defines incapacity (al-‘ajz) and refutes the Mu'tazili view of Abu Hashim. The author states that incapacity is the opposite (ḍidd) of power, not merely the absence of the property (malaka) of power as Abu Hashim claims. The evidence is the observed difference between a chronically ill person (zamin) who cannot stand due to a real inability, and a person who is prevented (mamnu‘) from acting due to external constraints, even though both lack the power to act. Abu Hashim might respond by denying incapacity in the prevented person, or by distinguishing that the prevented person has the potential for power while the ill person does not. The author then notes a consequence of considering incapacity as opposite: some transmitted from al-Ash'ari (the shaykh) that the object of incapacity is an existent thing, e.g., the chronically ill is 'incapable of sitting' meaning he has an attribute that entails sitting not due to power. But this is refuted by the certainty that the incapacity of those who challenge (mutahaddin) to produce like the Qur'an is about failing to bring something existent (the Qur'an), and if 'incapacity' is used for both the attribute and the lack of power, then homonymy is forced, which is against language. The majority (al-jumhur) hold that incapacity is a fixed accident opposite to power, because we are certain that in the chronically ill there is a meaning (a real quality) not found in the prevented person, even though both lack ability. In contrast, Abu Hashim considers it merely the absence of the property of power, with no real attribute in the chronically ill.

Key Terms
العجز
القدرة
أبو هاشم
الزمن
الممنوع
الملكة
الجمهور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Incapacity is merely the absence of power (i.e., lack of malaka).
Correction: The passage argues that incapacity is a real attribute opposite to power, as shown by the distinction between the chronically ill (who have an inherent inability) and the externally prevented (who lack power but do not have a positive attribute of incapacity).

Checkpoint
What is the majority view on incapacity (al-‘ajz)?
A) It is the absence of the property of power
B) It is a fixed accident opposite to power
C) It is a potential for power
D) It is a type of power

Key Takeaway
Incapacity is a real attribute opposite to power, not just the absence of power, as shown by the distinction between internal inability and external prevention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where you cannot do something, do you ever consider whether it is due to your own inherent incapacity or external prevention? How does recognizing your real incapacity before Allah affect your humility and reliance on Him?
?
The passage distinguishes between internal inability (zamin) and external prevention (mamnu‘). In your spiritual life, are you more likely to excuse yourself as 'prevented' by circumstances when you are actually revealing an inner incapacity to obey? What would it look like to honestly confront that?
?
If incapacity is a real attribute, what does it mean that we have no power except through Allah? How does this truth dismantle your pride in your own abilities, especially in acts of worship or good deeds? Will you today acknowledge a specific area where you are utterly incapable and turn to Him?

Definition of Sensory Pain (Waja) and Linguistic Nuances
Sanusi defines sensory pain as waja and clarifies its linguistic and conceptual scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars defined sensory pain (waja), its linguistic scope, and the philosophical distinction between sensory and intellectual pain.

Core Idea
Pain (waja) is a sensory perception, not an intellectual one.

Sanusi begins by clarifying that the term 'pain' (al-alam) in its sensory usage, especially tactile, is called waja. He then discusses the linguistic and philosophical dimensions, distinguishing sensory from intellectual pain and addressing potential objections. This lesson unpacks these foundational definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of waja
The passage opens by defining sensory pain, especially tactile, as waja. Note the specification 'siema al-lams' (especially tactile).
Ibn Sina's causes are mentioned but not detailed here.
2
Understand the linguistic usage of 'pain'
Sanusi explains that in language, 'pleasure' and 'pain' refer only to sensory experiences, not intellectual ones. In common usage, they are extended metaphorically to intellectual perceptions.
An objection is raised and refuted: intellectual perception of heat does not cause pain because the form is compatible.
3
Grasp the refutation of the objection
The refutation argues that the intellectual form of heat is compatible, while actual foreign heat is not perceived because it becomes natural. Therefore no pain.
This reinforces that pain is tied to sensory affect, not mere conception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: والحسي من الألم سيما اللمس يسمى وجعاً وحصر ابن سينا سبيه في تفرق الاتصال وسوء المزاج المختلف الحاد أو البارد لأن الرطب واليابس انفعاليات . نعم قد يؤلم اليابس بالعرض لاستتباعه لشدة التقبيض تفرق لاتصال بخلاف الرطب فإن ما يستعقبه من التمديد إنما هو بالمادة بخلاف المتفق ، أعني ما استقر في جوهر العضو وأبطال المقاومة وصار في حكم المزاج الأصلي، وذلك لأن شرط انفعال الحاسة عن المحسوس المخالفة في الكيف. لا شك أن لفظ اللذة أو الألم بحسب اللغة إنما هو للحسي دون العقلي ، وأما بحسب العرف فالظاهر أنه بحسب المعنوي حيث يؤخذ الإدراك أعم من الإحساس والتعقل، ولا يرد الاعتراض بأن المدقوق. قد يتعقل أن فيه حرارة غير طبيعية ولا يتألم لأن الحاصل (۲)) . بهذا التعقل صورة الحرارة المطابقة فهو إدراك [؟] ملائم لا مناف وإنما المنافي هوية الحرارة الغريبة وليست بمدركة، وإن كانت حاصلة لأنها صارت بمنزلة الطبيعة فلم يكن هناك انفعال وشعور فلم يكن ألم . وقيل : الاشتراك لفظي، والتفسير إنما هو للحسي خاصة، وأما الوجع فمختص بالحسي في العرف أيضاً ، بل الأظهر اختصاصه باللمس على ما صرح به البعض، وإن كان ظاهر كلام أئمة اللغة أنه يرادف الألم، فلذا قلنا : الحسي من الألم سيما اللمسي يسمى وجعاً. واتفقت كلمة الأطباء على أن كلا من تفرق الاتصال وسوء المزاج المختلف يقع سبباً للوجع في الجملة، وأن لا سبب له سواهما، إما بحكم الاستقراء، وإما بالاستدلال وإن كان ضعيفاً، وهو أن كمال العضو صحته وهي بالمزاج المعتدل، والهيئة التي بها تتأتى الأفعال على ما يجب. فالمنافي لهذا الكمال يكون مبطلاً لاعتدال المزاج، وهو سوء المزاج، أو للهيئة وهو تفرق الاتصال . وإنما اختلفوا في أن كلاً منهما يصلح سبباً بالذات كما يكون بالعرض وهو مذهب ابن سينا . أو السبب بالذات هو تفرق الاتصال فقط، وسوء المزاج إنما يكون سبباً بواسطة ما يلزمه من تفرق الاتصال وهذا هو المشهور من مذهب جالينوس وكثير من الأطباء، أو بالعكس أي السبب بالذات هو سوء المزاج فقط، والتفرق إنما يكون سبباً بواسطته. وإلى هذا مال الإمام الرازي وجمع من المتأخرين. وعلى كل من المذاهب احتجاجات واعتراضات أعرضنا عنها مخافة التطويل، وتفاصيلها في شرح القانون واشترط ابن سينا في سوء المزاج (۳)
He said: The sensory pain, especially tactile, is called waja. Ibn Sina confined its causes to disruption of continuity and bad temperament, whether hot or cold, because wet and dry are passivities. Indeed, dry may cause pain accidentally because it entails intense contraction leading to disruption of continuity, unlike wet, for the extension that follows it is only material, unlike that which is constant, i.e., what settles in the substance of the organ and nullifies resistance and becomes like the original temperament, because the condition for the sense organ's being affected by a sensed object is dissimilarity in quality. There is no doubt that the term pleasure or pain in language is only for the sensory, not the intellectual. But in common usage, it appears it is for the metaphorical, where perception is taken as more general than sensation and intellection, and the objection that the exact [scholar?] may intellectually conceive that there is unnatural heat in it and not be pained is not valid, because what is obtained by this intellection is a corresponding form of heat, so it is a perception, [missing?] compatible, not contrary. Rather, the contrary is the essence of the foreign heat, and it is not perceived, even if it exists, because it became like nature, so there was no affect or feeling, so there was no pain. It was said: the term is equivocal, and the definition is only for the sensory. As for waja, it is specific to the sensory in common usage as well, indeed the most apparent is its specificity to touch as stated by some, even if the apparent speech of the linguistic authorities is that it is synonymous with pain. Therefore we said: sensory pain, especially tactile, is called waja. The physicians agreed that both disruption of continuity and bad temperament are causes of waja in general, and that there is no cause other than them, either by induction or by reasoning, even if weak, which is: the perfection of an organ is its health, which is with balanced temperament, and the state by which actions are performed as they should. So what opposes this perfection either nullifies the moderation of temperament, which is bad temperament, or nullifies the state, which is disruption of continuity. They differed: are both essential causes as well as accidental? That is the school of Ibn Sina. Or is the essential cause only disruption of continuity, and bad temperament is only a cause by means of the disruption it necessitates? This is the famous school of Galen and many physicians. Or the opposite: the essential cause is only bad temperament, and disruption is only a cause by means of it. This is what Imam al-Razi and a group of later scholars inclined to. For each of these schools there are arguments and objections which we have omitted for fear of prolongation, and their details are in Sharh al-Qanun. And Ibn Sina stipulated in bad temperament...
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of physical pain (waja), its causes according to Ibn Sina, and the debate among physicians about essential vs accidental causality. The author starts by quoting that sensory pain, especially tactile, is called 'waja'. Ibn Sina limited its causes to two: disruption of continuity (tafarruq al-ittisal) and bad temperament (su' al-mizaj), whether hot, cold, dry, or wet. He notes that dry may cause pain accidentally due to inducing disruption of continuity, while wet may cause extension but materially. Then the author discusses linguistic usage: in language, 'pleasure' and 'pain' refer to sensory, not intellectual; in common usage, they are metaphorical, including intellectual perceptions. He refutes an objection that one may intellectually conceive of heat without pain—the intellectual form is compatible, while the actual foreign heat is not perceived, so no pain. He then mentions that the term is equivocal, and waja is specific to sensory, especially touch. Next, he reports the consensus of physicians that the two causes (disruption and bad temperament) are the sole causes of pain, either proven by induction or reasoning. The reasoning is based on the perfection of an organ being its health (balanced temperament and proper state). The three schools of thought on causality are: (1) Ibn Sina: both are essential causes; (2) Galen and many physicians: disruption is essential, bad temperament is accidental through causing disruption; (3) Imam al-Razi and later scholars: bad temperament is essential, disruption is accidental. The author chooses not to detail the arguments to avoid prolixity, referring interested readers to Sharh al-Qanun, and notes that Ibn Sina set certain conditions for bad temperament.

Key Terms
الوجع
الألم
اللذة
الإدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the term 'pain' (al-alam) primarily refers to intellectual or metaphorical pain in its original lexical meaning.
Correction: The passage clarifies that lexically, 'pain' and 'pleasure' refer to sensory experiences only; intellectual usage is metaphorical.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the term 'waja' specifically denote?
A) Any kind of pain, including intellectual
B) Sensory pain, especially tactile
C) Only chronic pain
D) Pain caused by bad temperament

Key Takeaway
Waja specifically denotes sensory pain, especially tactile, and is distinct from intellectual pain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a moment when you experienced severe physical pain. According to this lesson, the intellectual recognition of that pain is not itself pain. How can this distinction help you separate your spiritual state from your physical sensations?
?
The lesson restricts 'waja' to sensory tactile experiences. In light of this, what kind of pain do you think the soul experiences? And how should you respond to that pain differently from physical pain?

The Causes of Pain: Disruption of Continuity and Bad Temperament
Sanusi reports the physicians' consensus on the causes of pain and details three conflicting schools on essential causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two causes of pain agreed upon by physicians and the three schools of thought on whether they are essential or accidental causes.

Core Idea
Physicians agree on two causes of pain, but disagree on which is essential.

After defining waja, Sanusi presents the consensus of physicians that pain arises only from disruption of continuity (tafarruq al-ittisal) and bad temperament (su' al-mizaj). He then outlines three competing positions on the essential causality of these factors. This lesson explores these positions and their underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the consensus and the reasoning
The passage states that physicians agree that only these two causes exist, either through induction or through a reasoning based on organ perfection.
The reasoning defines health as balanced temperament and proper state; anything opposing it is either bad temperament or disruption.
2
Identify the three schools
The three schools are: (1) Ibn Sina: both are essential causes; (2) Galen: only disruption is essential; bad temperament is accidental via causing disruption; (3) Al-Razi: only bad temperament is essential; disruption is accidental.
Note the rationale for each position is not detailed here.
3
Note that details are omitted
Sanusi mentions that the arguments and objections for each school are omitted for brevity, referring to Sharh al-Qanun. He also notes Ibn Sina stipulated conditions for bad temperament.
This passage serves as a summary of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: والحسي من الألم سيما اللمس يسمى وجعاً وحصر ابن سينا سبيه في تفرق الاتصال وسوء المزاج المختلف الحاد أو البارد لأن الرطب واليابس انفعاليات . نعم قد يؤلم اليابس بالعرض لاستتباعه لشدة التقبيض تفرق لاتصال بخلاف الرطب فإن ما يستعقبه من التمديد إنما هو بالمادة بخلاف المتفق ، أعني ما استقر في جوهر العضو وأبطال المقاومة وصار في حكم المزاج الأصلي، وذلك لأن شرط انفعال الحاسة عن المحسوس المخالفة في الكيف. لا شك أن لفظ اللذة أو الألم بحسب اللغة إنما هو للحسي دون العقلي ، وأما بحسب العرف فالظاهر أنه بحسب المعنوي حيث يؤخذ الإدراك أعم من الإحساس والتعقل، ولا يرد الاعتراض بأن المدقوق. قد يتعقل أن فيه حرارة غير طبيعية ولا يتألم لأن الحاصل (۲)) . بهذا التعقل صورة الحرارة المطابقة فهو إدراك [؟] ملائم لا مناف وإنما المنافي هوية الحرارة الغريبة وليست بمدركة، وإن كانت حاصلة لأنها صارت بمنزلة الطبيعة فلم يكن هناك انفعال وشعور فلم يكن ألم . وقيل : الاشتراك لفظي، والتفسير إنما هو للحسي خاصة، وأما الوجع فمختص بالحسي في العرف أيضاً ، بل الأظهر اختصاصه باللمس على ما صرح به البعض، وإن كان ظاهر كلام أئمة اللغة أنه يرادف الألم، فلذا قلنا : الحسي من الألم سيما اللمسي يسمى وجعاً. واتفقت كلمة الأطباء على أن كلا من تفرق الاتصال وسوء المزاج المختلف يقع سبباً للوجع في الجملة، وأن لا سبب له سواهما، إما بحكم الاستقراء، وإما بالاستدلال وإن كان ضعيفاً، وهو أن كمال العضو صحته وهي بالمزاج المعتدل، والهيئة التي بها تتأتى الأفعال على ما يجب. فالمنافي لهذا الكمال يكون مبطلاً لاعتدال المزاج، وهو سوء المزاج، أو للهيئة وهو تفرق الاتصال . وإنما اختلفوا في أن كلاً منهما يصلح سبباً بالذات كما يكون بالعرض وهو مذهب ابن سينا . أو السبب بالذات هو تفرق الاتصال فقط، وسوء المزاج إنما يكون سبباً بواسطة ما يلزمه من تفرق الاتصال وهذا هو المشهور من مذهب جالينوس وكثير من الأطباء، أو بالعكس أي السبب بالذات هو سوء المزاج فقط، والتفرق إنما يكون سبباً بواسطته. وإلى هذا مال الإمام الرازي وجمع من المتأخرين. وعلى كل من المذاهب احتجاجات واعتراضات أعرضنا عنها مخافة التطويل، وتفاصيلها في شرح القانون واشترط ابن سينا في سوء المزاج (۳)

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience physical pain, do you immediately assume it is due to a disruption (injury) or an imbalance (illness)? How does your assumption affect your reaction and your reliance on Allah? This lesson shows that both are possible. Are you open to both possibilities, or do you instinctively favor one? What does that say about your trust in Allah's wisdom?
?
The three schools of physicians debated which cause is essential and which accidental. In your life, when you face trials (pain), do you ever stop to consider the 'essential cause' from a spiritual perspective? Could your 'bad temperament' (heart imbalance) be the essential cause of your suffering, with external disruptions being merely accidental? This lesson challenges you to examine your soul's state. Will you?
?
Ibn Sina held that both causes are essential and accidental. Al-Razi prioritized bad temperament. Galen prioritized disruption. Which school do you naturally lean towards in your own thinking? Why? And how might that bias affect your understanding of Allah's decree and your response to hardship? This passage invites you to question your own assumptions about causality.

Definition of Pain and Its Two Causes
Sanusi defines pain and waja', and states the two universal causes of pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Islamic scholars define pain and waja', and why they identify only two physical causes for it.

Core Idea
Pain has only two causes: structural disruption or temperament imbalance.

Before we can discuss deeper theological questions about suffering and divine justice, we need a precise understanding of what pain is physically. Sanusi draws from medical consensus to give a tight definition: pain arises from either a break in bodily continuity or a discordant change in temperament. This passage clarifies the terminology and the universal causes agreed upon by physicians.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Sanusi distinguishes between perceived and unperceived changes, and how he narrows the meaning of waja'.
Pay attention to the terms 'tashabuh' and 'munafi' – they set up the logic.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find where Sanusi defines 'alam' (pain) in general and then 'waja'' (its sensory, tactile subtype). Understand the relationship between them.
The passage distinguishes between sensory pain and non-sensory internal changes.
3
Understand the two causes
The text states that physicians agree on exactly two causes: tafarruq al-ittisal and su' al-mizaj al-mukhtalif. Note the proof given: health consists of balanced temperament and proper form; removing these causes pain.
The proof uses the concept of 'perfection of the organ'.
4
Reflect on the methodology
Sanusi mentions two ways this consensus is reached: induction (istiqra') and inference (istidlal). Consider why he calls the inference weak.
This shows the importance of empirical observation in medical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملائم لا، مناف وإنما المنافي هوية الحرارة الغريبة وليست بمدركة، وإن كانت حاصلة لأنها صارت بمنزلة الطبيعة فلم يكن هناك انفعال وشعور فلم يكن ألم . وقيل : الاشتراك لفظي، والتفسير إنما هو للحسي خاصة، وأما الوجع فمختص بالحسي في العرف أيضاً ، بل الأظهر اختصاصه باللمس على ما صرح به البعض، وإن كان ظاهر كلام أئمة اللغة أنه يرادف الألم، فلذا قلنا : الحسي من الألم سيما اللمسي يسمى وجعاً. واتفقت كلمة الأطباء على أن كلا من تفرق الاتصال وسوء المزاج المختلف يقع سبباً للوجع في الجملة، وأن لا سبب له سواهما، إما بحكم الاستقراء، وإما بالاستدلال وإن كان ضعيفاً، وهو أن كمال العضو صحته وهي بالمزاج المعتدل، والهيئة التي بها تتأتى الأفعال على ما يجب. فالمنافي لهذا الكمال يكون مبطلاً لاعتدال المزاج، وهو سوء المزاج، أو للهيئة وهو تفرق الاتصال .
Congruous not; rather the opponent is the identity of the foreign heat, and it is not perceived, even if it occurs, because it has become like a nature, so there was no passion and sensation, so there was no pain. And it was said: the shared meaning is verbal, and the interpretation is only for the sensory specifically; as for al-waja' (pain), it is specific to the sensory in common usage as well, rather the most apparent is its specificity to touch, as some have stated, although the apparent meaning of the language scholars is that it is synonymous with al-alam (pain). Hence we said: the sensory of pain, especially the tactile, is called waja'. And the agreement of the physicians is that each of tafarruq al-ittisal (discontinuity of continuity) and su' al-mizaj al-mukhtalif (discordant imbalance of temperament) is a cause of waja' in general, and that there is no cause other than them, either by induction, or by inference, although it is weak, namely that the perfection of an organ is its health, which is with a balanced temperament and the form by which actions occur as they should. So what opposes this perfection invalidates either the balance of temperament, which is su' al-mizaj, or the form, which is tafarruq al-ittisal.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage defines pain (al-alam) and its sensory type waja', and establishes the two causes universally accepted by physicians. The author begins by distinguishing between two kinds of opposing qualities: one that is not perceived because it becomes habitual (like internal heat), vs. one that is perceived and causes pain. He then notes that the word 'waja' is often used interchangeably with 'alam' but most precisely refers to tactile pain. The core teaching is that all physicians agree pain has only two causes: structural discontinuity (tafarruq al-ittisal) and a temperamental imbalance that is discordant (su' al-mizaj al-mukhtalif). The proof given is that an organ's perfection is its health, which consists in balanced temperament and proper structure; anything removing that perfection must attack either the balance or the structure. Thus pain arises from the perception of a deviation from the natural state.

Key Terms
ألم (alam)
وجع (waja')
تفرق الاتصال (tafarruq al-ittisal)
سوء المزاج (su' al-mizaj)
استقراء (istiqra')
استدلال (istidlal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pain is purely subjective and has no objective physical cause.
Correction: While pain is subjective experience, the passage argues it has specific physical causes: structural damage or temperament imbalance, as agreed by physicians.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two universal causes of pain (waja') as agreed upon by physicians?
A) Heat and cold
B) Tafarruq al-ittisal and su' al-mizaj al-mukhtalif
C) Wetness and dryness
D) Sensory and intellectual perception

Key Takeaway
Pain results from either structural damage (tafarruq al-ittisal) or a discordant humoral imbalance (su' al-mizaj al-mukhtalif), as deduced from the nature of health itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience pain, do you treat it as a mere inconvenience to be removed, or as a sign from your body that something is genuinely wrong? How does knowing that pain has only two specific causes change your response?
?
Consider a time you felt persistent discomfort without an obvious injury. Did you consider the possibility of a humoral imbalance? How does this lesson challenge you to take your body's signals more seriously and seek the root cause rather than just the symptom?
?
The passage describes pain as a departure from the perfection of health, which is a gift from Allah. When you are in pain, do you remember that your body is an amanah (trust)? What steps will you take this week to better attend to the signals your body sends?

Three Opinions on the Essential Cause of Pain
Sanusi enumerates three scholarly positions on the essential cause of pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct positions held by Ibn Sina, Galen, and al-Razi regarding which of the two known causes of pain is essential (bi al-dhat) and which is accidental (bi al-'arad).

Core Idea
Philosophers and physicians disagreed on whether pain's essential cause is discontinuity or imbalance.

Sanusi here presents a concise taxonomy of the main opinions among scholars on the essential cause of pain. The discussion revolves around two recognized causes: tafarruq al-ittisal (discontinuity of continuity) and su' al-mizaj (imbalance of temperament). The key question is which of these is essential and which is accidental. Three distinct positions are outlined, attributed to Ibn Sina, Galen, and al-Razi.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: it enumerates three views.
2
Identify the three positions
Find each scholar's view on whether the essential cause is discontinuity, imbalance, or both.
Ibn Sina says both can be essential; Galen says discontinuity only; al-Razi says imbalance only.
3
Understand the terminology
Ensure you are clear on the terms sabab bi al-dhat (essential cause) and sabab bi al-'arad (accidental cause).
The distinction hinges on whether a cause brings about its effect directly or through an intermediary.
4
Reflect on the reasoning
Consider why each scholar might have taken his position. Even though the text omits details, think about what logical or empirical considerations could lead to these differences.
Refer to the Commentary on the Canon for further details, as noted by Sanusi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما اختلفوا في أن كلاً منهما يصلح سبباً بالذات كما يكون بالعرض وهو مذهب ابن سينا . أو السبب بالذات هو تفرق الاتصال فقط، وسوء المزاج إنما يكون سبباً بواسطة ما يلزمه من تفرق الاتصال وهذا هو المشهور من مذهب جالينوس وكثير من الأطباء، أو بالعكس أي السبب بالذات هو سوء المزاج فقط، والتفرق إنما يكون سبباً بواسطته. وإلى هذا مال الإمام الرازي وجمع من المتأخرين. وعلى كل من المذاهب احتجاجات واعتراضات أعرضنا عنها مخافة التطويل، وتفاصيلها في شرح القانون
They only differed as to whether each of the two is a cause essentially (bi al-dhat) as it may be accidentally (bi al-'arad)—and this is the doctrine of Ibn Sina. Or the essential cause is only tafarruq al-ittisal, and su' al-mizaj is only a cause by means of what it entails of tafarruq al-ittisal—and this is the famous doctrine of Galen and many physicians. Or the reverse, i.e., the essential cause is only su' al-mizaj, and tafarruq al-ittisal is only a cause by means of it. This is what Imam al-Razi and a group of later scholars inclined to. And on each of the doctrines there are proofs and objections which we have refrained from mentioning for fear of prolongation; their details are in the Commentary on the Canon (Sharh al-Qanun).
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage enumerates the three main positions among physicians and philosophers regarding which of the two known causes of pain is essential (bi al-dhat) and which accidental (bi al-'arad). The classification is presented in an orderly fashion: (1) Ibn Sina's view that both can be essential or accidental depending on context; (2) Galen's view that only discontinuity is essential, and imbalance causes pain only because it leads to discontinuity; (3) al-Razi's view that only imbalance is essential, and discontinuity causes pain only because it involves imbalance. The author declines to recount the arguments and counter-arguments for each position, citing brevity and referring to the commentary on Ibn Sina's Canon for details.

Key Terms
سبب بالذات (sabab bi al-dhat)
سبب بالعرض (sabab bi al-'arad)
تفرق الاتصال (tafarruq al-ittisal)
سوء المزاج (su' al-mizaj)
مذهب ابن سينا
مذهب جالينوس
مذهب الرازي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all three views are equally arbitrary and lack reasoning.
Correction: In fact, each position is based on specific reasoning about the nature of causation and pain, as indicated by the existence of proofs and objections for each doctrine.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which scholar holds that the essential cause of pain is only tafarruq al-ittisal, and su' al-mizaj causes pain only by means of it?
A) Ibn Sina
B) Galen
C) Al-Razi
D) The author of the text

Key Takeaway
The passage summarizes three distinct views on the essential cause of pain, illustrating the nuanced debates among philosophers and physicians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience physical pain, which of these three views most closely matches your intuitive understanding of its cause? How does that shape your response to it?
?
Sanusi chose to omit the proofs and objections for each position to avoid prolongation. What does this teach you about the balance between depth and brevity in seeking knowledge? Are you prone to overcomplicating or oversimplifying important issues?
?
Imagine you are debating with a friend who insists that pain is always due to imbalance. Using the source, how would you respectfully show that there are other valid positions? What does this exercise reveal about your humility before differing scholarly opinions?

Ibn Sina's Two Conditions for Painful Imbalance
Sanusi explains and defends Ibn Sina's two conditions for painful imbalance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's two conditions for a painful organic imbalance: that it must involve an active quality (hot or cold) rather than a passive one (wet or dry), and that it must be discordant rather than uniform.

Core Idea
Painful imbalance requires active causes and discordance, not uniform deviation.

Ibn Sina's medical philosophy distinguishes between imbalances that cause pain and those that do not. This passage presents his two conditions: the imbalance must be due to active qualities (hot or cold) rather than passive ones (wet or dry), and it must be discordant (mukhtalif) rather than uniform (muttafiq). Understanding these conditions clarifies the causal mechanism of pain in Galenic-Avicennan medicine.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Familiarize yourself with the original text and its English rendering. Notice the structure: first condition, then discussion, then second condition.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify Ibn Sina's two conditions
The passage first states that the painful imbalance must be hot or cold, not wet or dry. Then it adds that it must be discordant, not uniform. Find these statements in the Arabic.
The second condition is at the very end: 'وأما الثاني...'
3
Understand the reasoning for excluding wet and dry
Ibn Sina argues that wetness and dryness are passive qualities, not active, so they cannot directly cause pain. But the passage discusses objections: dryness can cause pain indirectly by leading to 'tafarruq al-ittisal' (discontinuity), and wetness can too via extension, but only when accompanied by matter.
This shows the nuance: wet/dry can cause pain accidentally, not essentially.
4
Understand why uniform imbalance is painless
The second condition states that uniform imbalance (e.g., an evenly distributed fever) does not cause pain. The term 'al-muttafiq' literally means 'the uniform' and is used because it is painless.
This condition highlights that pain arises from opposition or contrariety.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واشترط ابن سينا في سوء المزاج المؤلم أن يكون حاراً ، أو بارداً ، لا رطباً أو يابساً، وأن يكون مختلفاً لا متفقاً . أما الأول : فلأن الرطوبة واليبوسة من الكيفيات الانفعالية دون الفعلية، وفيه بحث لأنه إن أريد أنهما ليست فاعليتين والمؤلم بالذات فاعل فيشكل بجعل اليبوسة سبباً لتفرق الاتصال وكليهما لكثير من الأمراض فليكونا سببين للوجع بذلك المعنى، من غير توسط تفرق الاتصال فلا ينحصر السبب فيه، وفي سوء المزاج الحار أو البارد. وأما السبب بالذات بمعنى المؤثر بالطبع فلا دليل على كون الحار والبارد وتفرق الاتصال كذلك، وإن أريد أن الوجع إحساس ما، والإحساس انفعال والانفعال لا يكون إلا عن فاعل وهما ليسا من الكيفيات الفاعلة فيشكل بتصريح ابن سينا في مواضع من كتبه بل إطباق القوم على أنهما من الكيفيات المحسوسة بل أوائل الملموسات فعند خروجهما عن الاعتدال يكونان متنافيين ، فإدراكهما من حيث هما كذلك يكون ألماً ثم ذكر ابن سينا أن : سوء المزاج اليابس قد يكون مؤلماً بالعرض لأنه قد يتبعه لشدة التقبيض تفرق الاتصال المؤلم بالذات . واعترض بأن الرطب أيضاً قد يستتبعه بواسطة التمديد اللازم لكثرة الرطوبة المحوجة إلى مكان أوسع وأجيب بأن ذلك إنما يكون في الرطوبة التي مع المادة فيكون الموجب هو المادة لا الرطوبة نفسها . وأما الثاني : فلأن سوء المزاج المتفق غير مؤلم، ولذلك يسمى بالمتفق
Ibn Sina stipulated in the painful imbalance (su' al-mizaj al-mu'lim) that it be hot or cold, not wet or dry, and that it be discordant, not uniform. As for the first: because wetness and dryness are among the passive qualities, not the active ones. But there is discussion: because if it is intended that they are not effective (fa'iliyya) while the essential cause of pain is effective, then this is problematic because dryness is made a cause of tafarruq al-ittisal, and both are causes of many diseases, so they would be two causes of waja' in that sense, without the mediation of tafarruq al-ittisal, so the cause is not confined to it and to hot or cold imbalance. And as for the essential cause in the sense of the natural effecter, there is no evidence that hot, cold, and tafarruq al-ittisal are such. And if it is intended that waja' is a kind of sensation, and sensation is a passion, and passion is only from an agent, and they (wet/dry) are not among the active qualities, then this is problematic because Ibn Sina explicitly states in several places of his books—indeed the consensus of the authorities—that they are among the sensible qualities, indeed among the first tangible things; so when they deviate from moderation they become contradictory, so perceiving them as such is pain. Then Ibn Sina mentioned that dry imbalance may be painful accidentally because it may be followed, due to strong constriction, by tafarruq al-ittisal which is painful essentially. And it was objected that wet also may be followed by it through the necessary extension due to the abundance of moisture requiring a wider space. And it was answered that this only occurs in moisture that accompanies matter, so the cause is the matter, not the moisture itself. As for the second: because uniform imbalance is not painful, and therefore it is called 'the uniform'.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage examines Ibn Sina's twofold condition for a painful imbalance: it must be a quality of heat or cold (not wetness or dryness), and it must be discordant (mukhtalif) rather than uniform (muttafiq). The author first addresses the rationale for excluding wetness and dryness: they are passive qualities, not active, so they cannot directly cause pain which is an effect. However, a discussion follows: if one takes 'essential cause' as the natural effecter, there is no proof that hot/cold and discontinuity are such either. Alternatively, if one considers pain as sensation (a passive response), then an agent is needed, but wet/dry are not active. This is countered by Ibn Sina's own acknowledgment that wet/dry are sensible and when extreme cause pain. The passage then resolves by noting that dryness can cause pain indirectly by leading to discontinuity via constriction, and wetness similarly via extension but only when accompanied by material substance, so the material, not the wetness per se, is the cause. The final point clarifies that uniform imbalance (e.g., an evenly distributed fever) does not cause pain, hence its name 'al-muttafiq'.

Key Terms
سوء المزاج المؤلم (su' al-mizaj al-mu'lim)
كيفية انفعالية (kayfiyya infi'aliyya)
كيفية فاعلة (kayfiyya fa'ila)
تفرق الاتصال (tafarruq al-ittisal)
بالعرض (bi al-'arad)
بالمتفق (al-muttafiq)
تقبيض (taqbiid)
تمديد (tamdid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All organic imbalances cause pain equally.
Correction: This passage shows that only imbalances that are discordant (mukhtalif) and involve active qualities (hot/cold) are directly painful. Uniform imbalances and those involving only wet/dry are not directly painful.

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn Sina require that the painful imbalance be hot or cold rather than wet or dry?
A) Because wet and dry are not sensible qualities
B) Because wet and dry are passive qualities, not active, and pain requires an active cause
C) Because hot and cold are more painful than wet and dry
D) Because the text does not give a reason

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's conditions refine the causal understanding of pain: only imbalances with active qualities (heat/cold) and discordance are painful; uniform imbalances and passive qualities cause pain only accidentally if at all.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a discomfort in your life—physical, emotional, or spiritual—that has become 'uniform', so familiar you no longer feel its pain. What would it take for you to recognize that this numbness is not health but a dangerous equilibrium, and what active change (hot or cold) is needed to break it?
?
When you experience pain, do you instinctively look for external, passive causes (like circumstances or other people) rather than the active, internal discord within your own heart or habits? How can this lesson's distinction between passive and active causes help you take responsibility for your own healing?

The Direct Causes of Pain: Active Qualities
al-Taftazani defines essentially painful qualities and discusses active vs passive causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani identifies heat and cold as direct causes of pain, and why moisture and dryness are not considered essentially painful.

Core Idea
Pain is sensed through active qualities: heat and cold, not passive ones.

This passage from al-Taftazani tackles a fundamental question: what qualities cause pain? He distinguishes between active qualities that directly produce pain and passive qualities that may be associated with pain only accidentally. The discussion sets the stage for a deeper exploration of causation in kalam and medicine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Read the opening statement: pain is from heat or cold, not moisture or dryness. Note the distinction between active and passive qualities.
This is the thesis al-Taftazani defends.
2
Follow the objection
The author presents an objection: if moisture/dryness are not active, how can they cause pain? He shows that they can cause pain accidentally through separation of continuity.
This clarifies the difference between direct and indirect causation.
3
Understand Ibn Sina's view
The passage cites Ibn Sina to argue that heat and cold are sensible qualities, and their perception when outside moderation is itself pain.
This supports the claim that they are direct causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المؤلم أن يكون حاراً، أو بارداً، لا رطباً أو يابساً، وأن يكون مختلفاً لا متفقاً. أما الأول: فلأن الرطوبة واليبوسة من الكيفيات الانفعالية دون الفعلية، وفيه بحث لأنه إن أريد أنهما ليست فاعليتين والمؤلم بالذات فاعل فيشكل بجعل اليبوسة سبباً لتفرق الاتصال وكليهما لكثير من الأمراض فليكونا سببين للوجع بذلك المعنى، من غير توسط تفرق الاتصال فلا ينحصر السبب فيه، وفي سوء المزاج الحار أو البارد. وأما السبب بالذات بمعنى المؤثر بالطبع فلا دليل على كون الحار والبارد وتفرق الاتصال كذلك، وإن أريد أن الوجع إحساس ما، والإحساس انفعال والانفعال لا يكون إلا عن فاعل وهما ليسا من الكيفيات الفاعلة فيشكل بتصريح ابن سينا في مواضع من كتبه بل إطباق القوم على أنهما من الكيفيات المحسوسة بل أوائل الملموسات فعند خروجهما عن الاعتدال يكونان متنافيين، فإدراكهما من حيث هما كذلك يكون ألماً ثم ذكر ابن سينا أن: سوء المزاج اليابس قد يكون مؤلماً بالعرض لأنه قد يتبعه لشدة التقبيض تفرق الاتصال المؤلم بالذات. واعترض بأن الرطب أيضاً قد يستتبعه بواسطة التمديد اللازم لكثرة الرطوبة المحوجة إلى مكان أوسع وأجيب بأن ذلك إنما يكون في الرطوبة التي مع المادة فيكون الموجب هو المادة لا الرطوبة نفسها. وأما الثاني: فلأن سوء المزاج المتفق غير مؤلم، ولذلك يسمى بالمتفق والمستوى حيث شابه المزاج الأصلي في عدم الإيلام، وذلك لأنه عبارة عن الذي استقر في جوهر العضو وأبطل المقاومة، وصار في حكم المزاج الأصلي فلا انفعال فيه للحاسة، فلا إحساس فلا ألم وأيضاً المنافاة إنما تتحقق بين شيئين فلا بد من بقاء المزاج الأصلي عند ورود الغريب ليتحقق إدراك كيفية منافية لكيفية العضو فيتحقق الألم، وأيضاً الدق أشد حرارة من الغب لأن الجسم الصلب لا يتسخن إلا عن حرارة قوية ولأنها تستعمل فيها مبردات أقوى مما يستعمل في الغب، ولأنها تؤدي إلى ذوبان مفرط من الأعضاء حتى الصلبة منها وصاحب الدق لا يجد من الالتهاب ما يجده صاحب الغب وما ذاك إلا لكون سوء المزاج المتفق لا يحس به. وأيضاً المستحم في الشتاء يشمئز بدنه عن الماء الفاتر ويتأذى به، ثم إنه بعد ذلك يستلذه ويستطيبه ثم إذا استعمل ماء حاراً تأذي به، ثم بعد ذلك يستلذه ثم إذا استعمل الماء الأول استبرده وتألم به، وذلك لما ذكرنا. واعلم أن سوء المزاج بل لا يدرك بالكلية وذلك إذا كان حدوثه بالتدريج فان الحادث منه أولاً يكون قليلاً جداً فلا يشعر به وبمنافاته، ثم في الزمان الثاني تكون الزيادة على تلك الحالة غير مشعور بها، وكذا في كل زمان. المتفق قد لا يوجع وهذا بخلاف ما يحدث دفعة لكثرته يكون مدركاً ثم يستمر إدراكه ما دام مختلفاً. قال: واعترض الامام الرازي بأن التفرق عدمي، وبأن من دوام الاغتذاء والتحلل تفرقاً كثيراً في الأعضاء، ولا ألم وبأن الألم قد يتأخر عن التفرق كما في
The painful [thing] is that it is hot or cold, not moist or dry, and that it is varied, not uniform. As for the first: because moisture and dryness are among the passive qualities, not the active ones, and there is investigation in it because if it is meant that they are not active and the essentially painful is active, then it is problematic by making dryness a cause for the separation of continuity, and both of them [cause] many diseases, so they would be two causes for pain in that sense, without the mediation of separation of continuity, so the cause is not confined to it, and in bad temperament whether hot or cold. And as for the cause in essence in the sense of the natural efficient cause, there is no evidence that heat, cold, and separation of continuity are such. And if it is meant that pain is a sensation, and sensation is a passion, and passion only comes from an agent, and these two are not among the active qualities, then it is problematic by the explicit statement of Ibn Sina in places in his books, rather the agreement of the people that they are among the sensible qualities, indeed the primary among tangible things, so when they depart from moderation they become opposed, so their perception as such is pain. Then Ibn Sina mentioned that dry bad temperament may be painful accidentally because it may be followed, due to the intensity of constriction, by separation of continuity which is essentially painful. And it was objected that moist also may be followed by it via the extension necessitated by the abundance of moisture requiring a wider space. And it was answered that this only occurs in moisture that is with matter, so the necessitating factor is matter, not moisture itself. And as for the second: because uniform bad temperament is not painful, and therefore it is called uniform and level when it resembles the original temperament in not causing pain. That is because it is that which has settled in the substance of the organ and nullified resistance, and has become in the ruling of the original temperament, so there is no passion in it for the sense, so no sensation, so no pain. Also, opposition only occurs between two things, so the original temperament must remain when the foreign [temperament] arrives for the perception of a quality opposed to the quality of the organ to be realized, and thus pain is realized. Also, dadd is hotter than ghubb because the hard body does not heat up except from strong heat, and because stronger coolants are used in it than those used in ghubb, and because it leads to excessive melting of the organs, even the solid ones, and the sufferer of dadd does not find from inflammation what the sufferer of ghubb finds, and that is only because uniform bad temperament is not sensed. Also, the bather in winter shudders at lukewarm water and is hurt by it, then after that he finds it pleasant and enjoys it, then if he uses hot water he is hurt by it, then after that he finds it pleasant, then if he uses the first water he finds it cold and suffers pain from it, and that is due to what we mentioned. And know that bad temperament is not perceived at all if it occurs gradually, for what occurs first is very little so he does not feel it or its opposition, then in the second time the increase upon that state is not perceived, and likewise in every time. The uniform may not cause pain, and this is contrary to what occurs suddenly, for due to its abundance it is perceived, then its perception continues as long as it is varied. He said: Imam al-Razi objected that separation is non-existent, and that from the continuity of nutrition and dissolution there is much separation in the organs without pain, and that pain may be delayed from separation, as in
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion by al-Taftazani on the causes of pain (al-alam). It opens with a thesis: pain is caused by heat or cold (active qualities) and by variation (ikhtilaf), not by moisture or dryness (passive qualities) nor by uniformity (ittifaq). The author then addresses each part. First, he explains that moisture and dryness are passive qualities, so they cannot be direct causes of pain. However, there is a discussion: if someone claims the term 'painful' applies only to an active quality, it is problematic because dryness can cause separation of continuity (tafarruq al-ittisal), which is painful. But the author clarifies that the direct cause in essence (al-sabab bi al-dhat) is the natural efficient cause, and there is no evidence that heat, cold, or separation are such. He then invokes Ibn Sina's agreement that heat and cold are sensible qualities, and their perception when outside moderation is painful. Ibn Sina also notes that dry temperament can be accidentally painful by causing separation. An objection is raised that moist temperament might also cause pain through expansion, but the answer is that this only occurs when moisture is combined with matter. Next, al-Taftazani discusses the second part: uniform bad temperament (saw' al-mizaj al-muttafiq) does not cause pain because it becomes like the original temperament and does not produce a passion in the sense. He supports this with examples: the difference between dadd (a type of fever) and ghubb (another type), and the bather who gradually adjusts to water temperature. He then notes that gradual onset of bad temperament is not perceived, while sudden change is. Finally, he records Imam al-Razi's objection that separation (tafarruq) is non-existent (adami) and thus cannot be a cause, and that pain may lag behind separation. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the main arguments are clear.

Key Terms
الكيفيات الفاعلة
الكيفيات الانفعالية
السبب بالذات
السبب بالعرض
تفرق الاتصال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All pain is caused by physical separation or injury.
Correction: Al-Taftazani argues that heat and cold themselves directly cause pain as sensible qualities, while separation (tafarruq) is only an accidental cause.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, which of the following is true about moisture and dryness?
A) They are direct causes of pain.
B) They are passive qualities that may cause pain accidentally.
C) They are active qualities that cause pain essentially.
D) They are not related to pain at all.

Key Takeaway
Heat and cold are essentially painful because they are active sensible qualities, while moisture and dryness are passive and only accidentally painful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience pain—whether from heat, cold, or dryness—do you ever pause to consider that it is a sign of your body's active qualities being out of balance? How does this awareness change your response to pain, or does it remain an automatic reaction?
?
This lesson teaches that some causes of pain are direct and essential, while others are accidental. In your spiritual life, are you more focused on the 'accidental' pains—those that come indirectly—or do you seek the essential causes that reveal deeper truths about yourself and your relationship with your Creator?
?
If pain is the perception of immoderate active qualities, then your body is a constant messenger of imbalance. What would it take for you to listen to that message not just with complaint but with a desire to restore moderation (wasatiyya) in your health, your habits, and your soul?

Why Uniform Bad Temperament Does Not Cause Pain
al-Taftazani explains why uniform bad temperament is not painful, with examples and an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani argues that uniform bad temperament (saw' al-mizaj al-muttafiq) does not produce pain, and how examples from fevers and bathing support this.

Core Idea
Uniformity in bad temperament cancels pain because it becomes like the original temperament.

This passage continues al-Taftazani's analysis of pain. After establishing that heat and cold cause pain when varied, he now explains why unvarying bad temperament—even if severe—does not produce sensation of pain. The discussion includes examples from different types of fevers and everyday experience, highlighting the importance of contrast in perception.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the concept of uniform vs varied temperament
Read the definition of uniform bad temperament and why it does not cause pain: it settles in the organ and nullifies resistance, becoming like the original.
This is the key principle.
2
Examine the examples
Look at the examples: the dadd and ghubb fevers, and the bather in water. See how they illustrate that uniform temperament is not sensed.
These examples make the abstract concept concrete.
3
Consider the objection
Al-Razi objects that separation (tafarruq) is non-existent and that pain can lag. Note how this objection relates to the earlier discussion of separation as a cause.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the main point is clear.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any bad temperament, regardless of uniformity, causes pain.

Checkpoint
Why does uniform bad temperament not cause pain, according to al-Taftazani?
A) It is milder than varied temperament.
B) It becomes like the original temperament and nullifies resistance.
C) It is too hot to be sensed.
D) It only affects solid organs.

Key Takeaway
Uniform bad temperament does not cause pain because it eliminates contrast with the original temperament, and gradual change goes unnoticed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The bather found the same water pleasant or painful depending on context. In your life, what 'waters' (habits, relationships, activities) have you come to see as pleasant that might actually be harmful, simply because you have adapted to them?

Causality and Created Things
