Gradual Change and Pain: The Imperceptible Shift
Explains why gradual su' al-mizaj is not perceived and sudden change is.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a gradual change in temperament is not felt as pain, while a sudden change is, and the underlying principles of perception and contrariety.

Core Idea
Gradual change is imperceptible; only sudden change triggers pain.

This lesson explores a fascinating aspect of medieval Islamic medicine and philosophy: why some changes in the body's temperament go unnoticed while others cause pain. The author provides both logical reasoning and vivid examples from everyday experience. By reading the passage, you will uncover the principle that gradual alterations escape sensory detection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to get a general sense of the argument.
Notice the use of medical terms like 'su' al-mizaj' and examples from fever and bathing.
2
Identify the key claim
Focus on the first sentence: 'the level where it resembles the original temperament in not causing pain.' This sets up the idea that some changes mimic the original state and thus don't cause pain.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the two examples: al-diq vs al-ghib fevers, and the bather in winter. Notice how each illustrates that consistent or gradual changes are not felt as painful.
The bather example shows adaptation and then contrast.
4
Understand the gradual vs sudden distinction
Read the final paragraph carefully: gradual change is imperceptible because the initial amount is minute and subsequent increments are too small to be noticed, unlike a sudden large change.
This is the core conclusion tying back to the learning objective.
5
Reflect on the philosophical implication
Consider why this matters for understanding pain and perception in general. How does this principle apply beyond medicine?
This connects to broader ideas in causality and epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمستوى حيث شابه المزاج الأصلي في عدم الإيلام، وذلك لأنه عبارة عن الذي استقر في جوهر العضو وأبطل المقاومة، وصار في حكم المزاج الأصلي فلا انفعال فيه للحاسة، فلا إحساس فلا ألم. وأيضاً المنافاة إنما تتحقق بين شيئين فلا بد من بقاء المزاج الأصلي عند ورود الغريب ليتحقق إدراك كيفية منافية لكيفية العضو فيتحقق الألم. وأيضاً الدق أشد حرارة من الغب لأن الجسم الصلب لا يتسخن إلا عن حرارة قوية ولأنها تستعمل فيها مبردات أقوى مما يستعمل في الغب، ولأنها تؤدي إلى ذوبان مفرط من الأعضاء حتى الصلبة منها وصاحب الدق لا يجد من الالتهاب ما يجده صاحب الغب وما ذاك إلا لكون سوء المزاج المتفق لا يحس به. وأيضاً المستحم في الشتاء يشمئز بدنه عن الماء الفاتر ويتأذى به، ثم إنه بعد ذلك يستلذه ويستطيبه ثم إذا استعمل ماء حاراً تأذي به، ثم بعد ذلك يستلذه ثم إذا استعمل الماء الأول استبرده وتألم به، وذلك لما ذكرنا. واعلم أن سوء المزاج لا يدرك بالكلية وذلك إذا كان حدوثه بالتدريج فان الحادث منه أولاً يكون قليلاً جداً فلا يشعر به وبمنافاته، ثم في الزمان الثاني تكون الزيادة على تلك الحالة غير مشعور بها، وكذا في كل زمان. المتفق قد لا يوجع. وهذا بخلاف ما يحدث دفعة لكثرته يكون مدركاً ثم يستمر إدراكه ما دام مختلفاً.
And the level where it resembles the original temperament in not causing pain, because it is that which has settled in the substance of the organ and nullified resistance, and become in the ruling of the original temperament, so there is no affection in it for the sense, thus no sensation, thus no pain. Also, contrariety only occurs between two things, so the original temperament must remain when the alien enters to realize the perception of a quality contrary to the quality of the organ, thus pain occurs. Also, al-diq is hotter than al-ghib because the solid body does not heat up except from intense heat, and because stronger coolants are used in it than those used in al-ghib, and because it leads to excessive melting of organs, even solid ones, and the sufferer of al-diq does not find the inflammation that the sufferer of al-ghib finds, and that is only because the consistent bad temperament is not sensed. Also, the bather in winter shudders at lukewarm water and is harmed by it, then afterwards he relishes and enjoys it, then if he uses hot water he is harmed by it, then afterwards he relishes it, then if he uses the first water he finds it cold and feels pain from it, and that is according to what we mentioned. And know that bad temperament is not perceived at all if it occurs gradually, for what occurs from it first is very slight and he is not aware of it or its contrariety, then in the second time the increase upon that state is not sensed, and likewise in every time. The consistent often does not cause pain. And this is contrary to what occurs suddenly, due to its abundance, it is perceived, then its perception continues as long as it is changing.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage explains why a gradual change in temperament (su' al-mizaj) is not felt as painful, while a sudden change is. It begins by describing a state where the temperament resembles the original (healthy) temperament in not causing pain because it has settled in the organ and nullified resistance, so the sense is unaffected. Then it notes that pain arises from contrariety between two things, so the original temperament must remain to perceive the alien quality. It gives examples: the 'diq' (a type of fever) is hotter than 'ghib' but does not feel as inflammatory because the bad temperament is consistent and not sensed; and a bather in winter initially dislikes lukewarm water but later enjoys it, then dislikes hot water, then when returning to lukewarm water finds it cold and painful. The core point is that gradual change is imperceptible because the initial change is minute and subsequent increments are too small to be detected, unlike a sudden large change which is immediately perceived.

Key Terms
سوء المزاج
الإيلام
الإحساس
المنافاة
الدق
الغب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any change in temperament necessarily causes pain.
Correction: Gradual changes in temperament are imperceptible and do not cause pain, because the initial change is minute and each subsequent increment is too small to be detected, unlike sudden large changes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a gradual change in temperament not cause pain?
A) Because it is too small at first and each increment is imperceptible.
B) Because the body becomes accustomed to it.
C) Because it is not a real change.
D) Because it does not affect the organ's form.

Key Takeaway
Gradual changes in temperament are imperceptible and do not cause pain, while sudden changes are perceived and cause pain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What gradual spiritual decay have you been ignoring because it doesn't cause immediate pain? Name one area where you have allowed a 'consistent bad temperament' to settle in your heart, and commit to one specific action today to address it.
?
The bather initially felt pain from lukewarm water, then adapted and enjoyed it. In what area of your life have you become accustomed to something that once troubled you? Is that adaptation a sign of growth or of desensitization to sin? What will you do to regain sensitivity?
?
This lesson warns that gradual changes can go undetected. Examine your habits over the past month: what small, imperceptible shifts have occurred in your devotion, honesty, or mercy? How will you prevent a sudden, painful awakening tomorrow?

Refuting al-Razi on Tafarruq as Cause of Pain
Refutes al-Razi's three objections to tafarruq causing pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic theologians refuted Imam al-Razi's objections that tafarruq (disruption of continuity) cannot be a cause of pain.

Core Idea
Even a non-existent attribute like tafarruq can be a preparatory cause of pain.

Imam al-Razi raised three powerful objections against the view that disintegration (tafarruq) causes pain. He argued that tafarruq is non-existent, that we experience constant internal disintegration without pain, and that pain can be delayed after a cut. In this passage, the author systematically refutes each objection, clarifying the nature of tafarruq as movement, the role of habituation, and the conditions for pain perception. Let's examine the refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation to grasp the flow of argument.
Notice the repetitive structure: objection then refutation.
2
Identify al-Razi's three objections
Find the three objections: (1) tafarruq is non-existent, (2) constant nutrition/dissolution causes tafarruq without pain, (3) pain can be delayed after tafarruq.
Each objection begins with 'ومنها' or 'بأن'.
3
Study the refutation of the first objection
The refutation argues that tafarruq is movement of parts, not privation. Even if non-existent, it can be a preparatory cause (mu'idd) rather than an efficient cause.
Key terms: حركة, عدمي, وجودي, المعد.
4
Examine the refutation of the second objection
Constant nutrition/dissolution involves small tafarruq that is habitual and not perceived; cumulative effect is needed for pain.
Habituation (الألف) and continuity (الاستمرار) are crucial.
5
Understand the refutation of the third objection
Pain delay occurs because a perceptible amount and conscious attention are required for pain to be felt.
The refutation is embedded in the first part: القدر المحسوس... مع الشعور والتفات النفس.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: واعترض الامام الرازي بأن التفرق عدمي، وبأن من دوام الاغتذاء والتحلل تفرقاً كثيراً في الأعضاء، ولا ألم وبأن الألم قد يتأخر عن التفرق كما في القطع بما هو في غاية الحدة. مدفوع بأن التفرق حركة بعض الأجزاء عن البعض على أن الممتنع سببية المعدوم دون العدمي خصوصاً في المدوم والمراد أن القدر المحسوس من التفرق إذا كان في عضو حاس مع الشعور والتفات النفس من غير ألف واستمرار وقد أدرك من جمعه كونه منافياً فهو مؤلم، ولو بواسطة استتباعه فقدان هيئة العضو كماله اللائق وحينئذ لا إشكال. إشارة إلى دفع الشبه التي أوردها الإمام على كون تفرق الاتصال سبباً للوجع، فمنها: أن التفرق يرادف الانفصال وهو عدمي فلا يصلح علة للوجع لأنه وجودي. وأجيب أن الانفصال المرادف للتفرق ليس هو عدم الاتصال بل حركة بعض الأجزاء عن البعض فلا يكون عدمياً، ولو سلم فلا محالة يلزمه كون هيئة العضو فاقدة كماله اللائق به وأمكن إدراكه من هذه الجهة فيكون موجعاً بذاته بمعنى أنه ليس بتوسط سوء المزاج، وإن كان بتوسط ما يلزمه من خروج الهيئة العضوية عن كمالها. ولو سلم فالعدمي لا يلزم أن يكون معدوماً ليمتنع كونه علة للوجودي، ولوسلم فالمراد بالسبب ها هنا المعد أي الفاعل لإعداد العضو لقبول الوجع لا المؤثر الموجد، ولا امتناع في أن يكون التفرق العدمي بحيث متى حصل اقتضى الوجع كسوء المزاج. ومنها أنه: لو كان سبباً للوجع لكان الإنسان دائماً في الوجع لأنه دائماً في تفرق الاتصال بواسطة الاغتذاء والتحلل، لأن الاغتذاء والنمو إنما يكون بنفوذ الغذاء في الأعضاء والتحلل إنما يكون بانفصال أجزاء عن الأعضاء. لا يقال: هذا التفرق لكونه في غاية الصغر لا يؤلم أو لا يحس بألمه لا سيما وقد صار مألوفاً بدوامه. لأنا نقول: كل تفرق وإن كان صغيراً لكن جملتها كثيرة جداً، ولو كان التفرق ما كان مألوفاً غير مؤلم لكان تفرق كذلك، لأن حكم الأمثال واحد. ومنها: أن التفرق لو كان سبباً بالذات لما تأخر عنه الأثر بحسب الزمان.
He said: Imam al-Razi objected that tafarruq (disruption) is non-existent, and that from the continuity of nutrition and dissolution there is much tafarruq in the organs with no pain, and that pain may be delayed from tafarruq as in cutting with something extremely sharp. It is refuted by saying that tafarruq is a movement of some parts away from others, insofar as it is impossible for a non-existent (ma'dum) to be a cause, but not for a non-existent attribute (adami), especially in that which is continuous. And the meaning is that the perceptible amount of tafarruq, if it is in a sensing organ with consciousness and attention of the soul, without habituation and continuity, and it has been perceived from its entirety that it is contrary, then it causes pain, even if by way of its entailing the loss of the organ's form of its due perfection; and then there is no problem. This is an indication of refuting the doubts that the Imam brought forward concerning tafarruq of connection being a cause of pain. Among them: that tafarruq is synonymous with separation (infisal) which is non-existent, so it is not valid as a cause of pain because pain is existent. It was answered that separation synonymous with tafarruq is not the absence of connection but rather a movement of some parts away from others, so it is not non-existent. And if it is granted, then inevitably it entails that the form of the organ is lacking its due perfection, and it could be perceived from this aspect, so it would be painful in itself, meaning not through the intermediacy of bad temperament, even if it is through the intermediacy of what it entails of the exit of the organic form from its perfection. And if it is granted, then the non-existent attribute does not necessarily have to be non-existent to be prevented from being a cause of the existent. And if it is granted, then the meaning of cause here is the preparer (mu'idd), i.e., the agent which prepares the organ to accept pain, not the effective agent (mu'aththir). And there is no impossibility in the non-existent tafarruq being such that whenever it occurs, it necessitates pain, like bad temperament. And among them: that if it were a cause of pain, then man would always be in pain because he is always in tafarruq of connection through nutrition and dissolution, since nutrition and growth occur only by the penetration of food into the organs, and dissolution occurs only by the separation of parts from the organs. It cannot be said: this tafarruq, because it is extremely small, does not cause pain or its pain is not felt, especially since it has become habitual by its continuity. Because we say: every tafarruq, even if small, but its total is very much; and if tafarruq were such that it was habitual and not painful, then similarly tafarruq would be, because the judgment of like things is one. And among them: that if tafarruq were a cause in itself, its effect would not be delayed in time.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's objection that tafarruq (disintegration) cannot be a cause of pain, along with a detailed refutation. The objection has three points: (1) tafarruq is non-existent (adami), so it cannot cause pain which is existent; (2) constant nutrition and dissolution involve tafarruq in organs yet no pain occurs; (3) pain can be delayed after tafarruq, as with a very sharp cut. The refutation first argues that tafarruq is a movement of parts, not a privation, so it is existent. Even if it were non-existent, it could still be a preparatory cause (mu'idd) rather than an efficient cause. The text also addresses the objection about constant pain: although individual tafarruq events are small, their cumulative effect can be significant. And the delay issue is explained by the need for a perceptible amount and attention. The passage systematically defends the view that tafarruq of continuity is a cause of pain.

Key Terms

العدمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only existent things can be causes.
Correction: Non-existent attributes (adami) can serve as preparatory causes (mu'idd) for existent effects, as clarified in the refutation.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute al-Razi's claim that tafarruq is non-existent and cannot cause pain?
A) By arguing that tafarruq is a movement of parts, not a privation.
B) By denying that tafarruq occurs in the body.
C) By claiming that pain itself is non-existent.
D) By admitting that only existent things can be causes.

Key Takeaway
Tafarruq, even if considered non-existent, can serve as a preparatory cause for pain, contrary to al-Razi's claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a potential 'cause' in your spiritual life simply because it seemed intangible or 'non-existent'? What subtle, imperceptible changes in your heart might be preparing you for either pain or healing? Will you commit to examining those small movements today?
?
This lesson shows that even small, repeated tafarruq can accumulate unnoticed until pain becomes unavoidable. In your relationship with Allah, what small negligence or sin have you normalized through habit? What one concrete step can you take right now to break that habit before it escalates?
?
Al-Razi assumed that since tafarruq is non-existent, it cannot cause existent pain. Are there areas in your faith where you reject a truth because it doesn't fit your initial framework? How can you cultivate the humility to accept refutations from the Quran and Sunnah even when they challenge your logic?

Refuting the First Objection Against Disconnection as Cause of Pain
Al-Taftazani refutes the objection that non-existent disconnection cannot cause existent pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Taftazani refutes the objection that a non-existent disconnection cannot be the cause of existent pain, by distinguishing between absence of connection and motion, and between preparatory and efficient causes.

Core Idea
Even non-existent things can be preparatory causes for existent effects.

In this passage, Al-Taftazani tackles a key philosophical objection: how can non-existent disconnection cause real pain? He presents a multi-layered refutation, showing the objection fails on several grounds. You will see the careful distinctions he makes between motion and absence, and between types of causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overview of the argument structure.
Pay attention to the repeated 'If it is conceded' (wa law sallama) phrases that mark each layer.
2
Identify the objection
The objector claims that disconnection (tafarruq) is non-existent, so it cannot cause existent pain. Note the terms 'adami (non-existent) and wujudi (existent).
3
Trace the four layers of the answer
Al-Taftazani gives four responses, each building on the previous. Identify each: (1) disconnection is motion, not absence; (2) even if non-existent, it entails deprivation; (3) non-existent can be a cause; (4) 'cause' here means preparatory, not efficient.
The answers are linked by the phrase 'And if it is conceded'.
4
Reflect on the preparatory vs. efficient cause distinction
Understand the key distinction between mu'idd (preparatory cause) and mu'aththir (efficient cause). The author argues that a non-existent state like disconnection or bad temperament can prepare the organ to receive pain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إشارة إلى دفع الشبه التي أوردها الإمام على كون تفرق الاتصال سبباً للوجع ، فمنها : أن التفرق يرادف الانفصال وهو عدمي فلا يصلح علة للوجع لأنه وجودي . وأجيب أن الانفصال المرادف للتفرق ليس هو عدم الاتصال بل حركة بعض الأجزاء عن البعض فلا يكون عدمياً، ولو سلم فلا محالة يلزمه كون هيئة العضو فاقدة كماله اللائق به وأمكن إدراكه من هذه الجهة فيكون موجعاً بذاته بمعنى أنه ليس بتوسط سوء المزاج ، وإن كان بتوسط ما يلزمه من خروج الهيئة العضوية عن كمالها . ولو سلم فالعدمي لا يلزم أن يكون معدوماً ليمتنع كونه علة للوجودي ، ولوسلم فالمراد بالسبب ها هنا المعد أي الفاعل لإعداد العضو لقبول الوجع لا المؤثر الموجد، ولا امتناع في أن يكون التفرق العدمي بحيث متى حصل اقتضى الوجع كسوء المزاج.
An indication to rebut the doubts that the Imam brought on that disconnection of continuity is a cause of pain. Among them: that disconnection is synonymous with separation, and it is non-existent, so it cannot be a cause of pain because pain is existent. And it was answered that separation synonymous with disconnection is not the absence of connection but rather movement of some parts away from others, so it is not non-existent. And if it is conceded, then necessarily it entails that the state of the organ lacks its proper perfection, and it can be perceived from this aspect, so it is painful in essence in the sense that it is not by means of bad temperament, even if it is by means of what entails the departure of the organic state from its perfection. And if it is conceded, the non-existent is not necessary to be absolutely non-existent such that it is impossible for it to be a cause of the existent. And if it is conceded, what is meant by 'cause' here is the preparatory (mu'idd), i.e., the agent preparing the organ for the reception of pain, not the effective cause (mu'aththir). And there is no impossibility in the non-existent disconnection being such that whenever it occurs it necessitates pain, like bad temperament.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. Al-Taftazani begins by introducing the context: he is rebutting doubts raised by the Imam (al-Razi or al-Ghazali) against the claim that disconnection of continuity (tafarruq al-ittisal) is a cause of pain. The first objection is that disconnection is synonymous with separation (infisal), which is non-existent ('adami); pain is existent (wujudi), so a non-existent thing cannot cause an existent thing. The answer has multiple layers. First, the separation meant here is not absence of connection but movement of parts away from others—this is a motion, which is existent, so the objection fails. Second, even if we concede it is non-existent, disconnection still entails the organ losing its proper perfection, and this deprivation can be perceived and cause pain directly without bad temperament. Third, even if we concede further that it is non-existent, a non-existent thing can still be a preparatory cause (mu'idd) preparing the organ to receive pain, like how bad temperament (su' al-mizaj) is a non-existent state that disposes the body to pain. The author carefully distinguishes between a preparatory cause and an efficient cause (mu'aththir), and argues that there is no impossibility in the non-existent being a preparatory cause.

Key Terms
تفرق
انفصال
عدمي
وجودي
موجع بالذات
سوء المزاج
معد
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A non-existent thing cannot be a cause of anything.
Correction: A non-existent thing can be a preparatory cause (mu'idd) that disposes a subject to receive an effect, even if it is not an efficient cause.

Checkpoint
What is the primary distinction made in the answer to the first objection regarding the nature of disconnection?
A) Disconnection is non-existent so it cannot cause pain.
B) Disconnection is motion of parts, not absence of connection, so it is existent.
C) Disconnection is always existent because it involves movement.
D) Disconnection is a type of bad temperament.

Key Takeaway
Disconnection causes pain not as an efficient cause but as a preparatory state that disposes the organ to pain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience physical pain, do you ever stop to reflect that its ultimate cause is Allah, or do you get absorbed in the intermediary preparatory causes like bad temperament? How can this lesson's distinction between efficient and preparatory causes deepen your reliance on Allah alone?
?
This lesson argues that even a non-existent state can be a preparatory cause for pain. How often do you attribute your suffering to immediate physical causes while neglecting that Allah is the sole Creator of all effects? What would change in your patience and gratitude if you truly internalized this?
?
The author shows that pain can result from the perception of a lack of perfection. In what areas of your life do you feel pain due to perceived deficiency? How can recognizing that this pain is a created trial draw you closer to Allah, rather than leading to despair or blame of secondary causes?

Second Objection: Constant Disconnection and Pain
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the second objection about constant disconnection causing constant pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against disconnection as a cause of pain and its refutation through the principle that similar things have the same ruling.

Core Idea
If disconnection caused pain, we would always be in pain—but we are not, so disconnection is not the cause.

This lesson presents a second objection raised against the theory that disconnection (tafarruq al-ittisal) is the cause of pain. The objection argues that if disconnection were a cause, then bodily processes of nutrition and decomposition would cause constant pain. The response anticipates a counterargument about small disconnections becoming habitual, and refutes it by invoking the logical principle that similar things have the same ruling.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening lines of the passage carefully. Notice how the objection assumes that disconnection is constant due to nutrition and decomposition.
2
Identify the counterargument
Find the part where a potential objection is raised that small and habitual disconnections do not cause pain. This is a common defense.
The author uses 'la yiqalu' to introduce the counterargument.
3
Analyze the refutation
Study how the author refutes the counterargument: by noting that the totality of small disconnections is large, and by applying the principle that similar things have the same ruling.
The principle 'hukm al-amthal wahid' is key.
4
Reflect on the logical force
Consider how this refutation strengthens the overall argument that disconnection is not the cause of pain. The objection fails because it cannot account for the absence of constant pain.
This prepares for the definitive refutation in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنه : لو كان سبباً للوجع لكان الإنسان دائماً في الوجع لأنه دائماً في تفرق الاتصال بواسطة الاغتذاء والتحلل، لأن الاغتذاء والنمو إنما يكون بنفوذ الغذاء في الأعضاء والتحلل إنما يكون بانفصال أجزاء عن الأعضاء. لا يقال : هذا التفرق لكونه في غاية الصغر لا يؤلم أو لا يحس بألمه لا سيما وقد صار مألوفاً بدوامه . لأنا نقول : كل تفرق وإن كان صغيراً لكن جملتها كثيرة جداً، ولو كان التفرق ما كان مألوفاً غير مؤلم لكان تفرق كذلك، لأن حكم الأمثال واحد.
And among them is: that if it were a cause of pain, then man would always be in pain because he is always in disconnection of continuity by way of nutrition and decomposition. For nutrition and growth occur only by penetration of food into the organs, and decomposition occurs only by separation of parts from the organs. It cannot be said: this disconnection, because it is extremely small, does not cause pain or its pain is not felt, especially since it has become habitual by its constancy. For we say: every disconnection, even if small, yet their totality is very great. And if the disconnection was habitual and non-painful, then every disconnection would be like that, because the ruling of similar things is one.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its answer. The objection reasons: if disconnection were a cause of pain, then a person would always be in pain because the body is constantly undergoing disconnection through nutrition (ightidha') and decomposition (tahallul). Nutrition requires food penetrating organs and separating them, and decomposition involves parts detaching. The answer anticipates a potential counterargument: one might say that these disconnections are extremely small and have become habitual, so they do not cause pain or are not felt. The author refutes this by arguing that although each disconnection is small, their totality is very large, and if a habitual disconnection were considered non-painful, then all disconnections would be ruled similarly, which is false because the ruling of similar things is one. This sets up a logical challenge that will be answered more fully in the next lesson.

Key Terms
اغتذاء
تحلل
مألوف
صغر
حكم الأمثال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All disconnections are equally painful regardless of size or habit.
Correction: The commentator notes that totality matters (small disconnections accumulate) and that habit cannot nullify the principle that similar things have the same ruling.

Checkpoint
What is the objection's argument regarding constant disconnection?
A) It proves that disconnection is not a cause of pain because pain is not constant.
B) It claims that constant disconnection from nutrition would cause constant pain if disconnection were a cause.
C) It argues that small disconnections are not felt, so they cannot cause pain.
D) It states that habitual disconnections are painless.

Key Takeaway
The second objection fails because the principle of similar rulings prevents habit from nullifying pain, and the totality of small disconnections cannot be ignored.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the small, habitual sins or spiritual lapses you dismiss as 'too minor to matter.' How does the argument about the cumulative totality of small disconnections challenge your tolerance of these? What would it mean for you to treat each small lapse as significant?
?
The objection claimed that constant disconnection doesn't cause pain because it's habitual. In your life, are you numb to any constant spiritual or moral disconnection—like neglect of prayer or harsh words at home—because you've grown used to it? What 'pain' are you ignoring, and how can you reawaken your sensitivity?
?
The principle of similar rulings demands consistency: if you excuse a fault in yourself because it's 'habitual,' would you excuse the same fault in others? Where are you inconsistent, and what would it take for you to apply the same standard to yourself and others?

Clarifying Essential Causality: Pain and Quick Cutting
Al-Taftazani explains that pain does not necessarily follow disconnection instantly; it requires perception and attentiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the delay of pain after a quick cut does not disprove essential causality, and what specific conditions make disconnection painful by essence.

Core Idea
Essential cause does not mean immediate; pain arises from perceived, non-habitual disconnection.

This passage addresses a common objection against the view that disconnection causes pain essentially. The objector argues that if disconnection were an essential cause, pain would follow instantly, but a quick cut shows a delay. Al-Taftazani's joint answer refines what 'essential cause' means, showing that pain requires specific conditions: perception, attention, non-habitual nature, and perceived contrariety. This lesson unpacks that answer.

Guided Path
1
Understand the third objection
Read the objector's argument: if disconnection were an essential cause, pain would not lag. But quick cutting shows pain only after a moment.
This objection uses the principle of necessary concomitant.
2
Grasp the refined meaning of 'cause by essence'
The author clarifies that 'cause by essence' does not mean the complete cause. Disconnection is painful by essence only when certain conditions are met.
The complete cause would include perception and attention.
3
Identify the four conditions for pain
List the conditions: (1) perceived amount of disconnection, (2) in a sensitive organ, (3) with attention and perception, (4) not habitual and perceived as contrary to the organ's quality.
These conditions explain why nutritional disconnection does not cause pain.
4
Apply the explanation to the quick cut case
If the soul attends to the cut, pain is immediate; if distracted, no pain occurs. This resolves the objection.
The author gives examples of distraction (scientific contemplation, games).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها: أن التفرق لو كان سبباً بالذات لما تأخر عنه الأثر بحسب الزمان . واللازم باطل لأن قطع العضو بآلة في غاية الحدة قطعاً في غاية السرعة لا يحس منه بالألم إلا بعد لحظة . ريثما يحصل سوء المزاج. وجوابهما : أنا لا نعنى بكون تفرق الاتصال سبباً للوجع بالذات أنه نفسه تمام العلة بحيث لا يتخلف الوجع عنه أصلاً ، بل نعني أن القدر المحسوس من التفرق إذا كان في عضو حاس مع التفات النفس إليه والشعور به من غير أن يكون مستمراً مألوفاً. ويشترط أن يدرك من جهة كونه منافياً لكيفية العضو فهو مؤلم بالذات بمعنى عدم التوقف على سوء المزاج، وإن كان إيلامه بواسطة ما يلزمه من فقدان هيئة العضو كماله اللائق به وحينئذ يجوز أن لا يكون التفرق في الاغتذاء والتحلل قدر ما يدركه الحس، أو يكون مألوفاً لا يضر ولا يؤلم أو يكون إدراكه لا من جهة كونه منافياً وتفرقاً ، بل من جهة كونه ملائماً ونافعاً للبدن ببقاء الصحة والقسوة وبقاء البدن من الفضول. وما ذكر من لزوم استواء التفرقات في الأحكام ظاهر الفساد : كيف والتفرق الغذائي طبيعي دائم في أجزاء صغيرة مألوف يترتب عليه للبدن مصالح كثيرة وقطع العضو ليس كذلك وأما قطع العضو سريعاً بآلة في غاية الحدة فإن كان مع التفات النفس والشعور فلا نسلم تأخر الألم. وإن كانت بدونه فلا إشكال. ألا ترى أن من انصرف فكرته إلى أمر مهم شريف كالتأمل في مسألة علمية ، أو خسيس كاللعب بالنرد والشطرنج ، أو بتوسط كالابتلاء
And among them is: that if disconnection were a cause by essence, the effect would not be delayed with respect to time. The consequent is false, because cutting a limb with a tool of extreme sharpness, a cut of extreme speed, pain is not felt from it except after a moment, until bad temperament occurs. And the answer to both is: we do not mean by disconnection of continuity being a cause of pain by essence that it itself is the complete cause such that pain never lags behind it at all. Rather, we mean that the perceived amount of disconnection, when it is in a sensitive organ, with the attention of the soul towards it and perception of it, without it being continuous and habitual, and it is perceived from the aspect of being contrary to the quality of the organ, then it is painful by essence in the sense of not being dependent on bad temperament, even if its infliction of pain is by means of what it entails of losing the state of the organ of its proper perfection. And in that case, it is possible that the disconnection in nutrition and decomposition is not of a magnitude that the sense can perceive, or it is habitual and does not harm or cause pain, or its perception is not from the aspect of being contrary and disconnection, but rather from the aspect of being suitable and beneficial to the body in preserving health and firmness and ridding the body of wastes. And what was mentioned of the necessity of equality of disconnections in rulings is clearly invalid: how? For the nutritional disconnection is natural, continuous, in small parts, habitual, and from it follow many benefits for the body, and cutting a limb is not like that. And as for cutting a limb quickly with a tool of extreme sharpness, if it is with attention of the soul and perception, then we do not concede the delay of pain. And if it is without that, then there is no problem. Do you not see that the one whose thought is diverted to a matter important and noble, like contemplation of a scientific question, or base like playing backgammon and chess, or intermediate like trial...
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and a joint answer that also resolves the second objection. The third objection is: if disconnection were an essential cause (bi al-dhat), the effect (pain) would not be delayed in time. But a quick cut with a sharp tool produces pain only after a moment (while bad temperament develops), so this delay disproves essential causality. The answer ('we answer both' referring to the second and third objections) clarifies that 'cause by essence' does not mean that disconnection is the complete cause (al-'illah al-tammah) from which pain never lags. Rather, the disconnection that is painful is the perceived amount (al-qadr al-mahsus) in a sensitive organ ('udw hass) with the soul's attention and perception, without it being continuous and habitual, and it is perceived as contrary (munafi) to the organ's quality. This kind of disconnection is painful by essence (mu'alim bi al-dhat) in the sense that it does not require bad temperament, though its painfulness may be mediated by the loss of the organ's proper state. This explanation also handles the second objection: disconnections from nutrition and decomposition are either imperceptibly small, or habitual, or perceived as beneficial (mulayim wa nafi') for health, so they do not cause pain. The author then dismisses the claim that all disconnections must be equal in their ruling, contrasting nutritional disconnection (natural, continuous, small, habitual, beneficial) with cutting a limb. Regarding the quick cut, if the soul attends to it, pain is immediate; if distracted (e.g., by thinking about a scientific problem or playing games), no pain occurs. The passage ends mid-example.

قدر محسوس
عضو حاس
التفات النفس
منافٍ
ملائم
مألوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'essential cause' means the cause always and immediately produces its effect without any mediating conditions.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'cause by essence' in this context means that the disconnection is painful due to its intrinsic nature, but it still requires facilitating conditions like perception, attention, and non-habitual status for the effect to occur.

Checkpoint
According to the joint answer, what conditions are necessary for disconnection to be painful?
A) It must be in any organ, continuous, and habitual.
B) It must be perceived in a sensitive organ with attention, not continuous and not habitual, and perceived as contrary to the organ's quality.
C) It must be caused by bad temperament.
D) It must be large in magnitude and sudden.

Key Takeaway
Disconnection causes pain essentially only when it is perceived in a sensitive organ with attention, not habitual, and perceived as contrary; this explains both the quick cut case and why nutritional disconnection does not cause pain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience pain, do you ever pause to consider that your soul's attention and perception play a role in its intensity? How might shifting your focus away from pain—whether through distraction or deeper reflection—change your experience of it, and what does that tell you about the connection between your spiritual state and your physical sensations?
?
This lesson teaches that habitual, beneficial processes in your body do not cause pain, while sudden, contrary acts do. Examine your own life: what 'disconnections'—such as sins or unhealthy habits—have become so habitual that you no longer feel their pain? How can you reawaken your soul's perception to see them as contrary to your fitrah?
?
Al-Taftazani mentions that someone engrossed in a noble thought may not feel a cut. Is there a 'noble thought' or spiritual focus that so occupies your heart that it numbs the pains of this world? If not, what is distracting you from that level of presence, and what steps will you take today to cultivate it?

Refutation of the Claim That Pain from Disconnection Is Delayed
Objection and answer about the causality of pain from disconnection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced causal relationship between disconnection of continuity and pain, including the roles of perception, attention, and habituation.

Core Idea
Disconnection causes pain only when perceived as contrary to the organ's proper state.

The objector argues that if disconnection were the direct cause of pain, cutting should cause immediate pain, but it is felt only after a moment. The author clarifies that disconnection is not the sole cause; pain depends on the organ's sensitivity, the soul's attention, and whether the disconnection is perceived as harmful. He then distinguishes between types of disconnection and provides an example of distraction to illustrate that attention affects pain perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the objection that cutting with a sharp tool delays pain, and the author's immediate answer that disconnection is not the complete cause.
Notice the objector's assumption that pain must follow disconnection instantly.
2
Understand the nuanced definition of the cause
The author explains that pain arises when the sensed disconnection is in a sensitive organ, the soul attends to it, and it is perceived as contrary to the organ's quality.
Pain depends on more than just physical disconnection.
3
Distinguish types of disconnection
Compare nutritional disconnection (habitual, beneficial, painless) with cutting a limb (non-habitual, harmful, painful). This refutes the claim that all disconnections are equal.
Habit and benefit affect pain perception.
4
Consider the example of distraction
The author uses the example of a person absorbed in thought to show that without attention, pain (e.g., hunger) may not be felt.
This reinforces that attention is necessary for pain.

And the necessary consequence is false because cutting a limb with a tool of extreme sharpness in an extremely quick cutting does not give sensation of pain except after a moment, until a bad temperament occurs. And their answer: we do not mean that disconnection of continuity is a cause of pain essentially such that it itself is the complete cause so that pain never lags behind it at all, but we mean that the sensed quantity of disconnection, when it is in a sensitive organ with the soul's attention to it and awareness of it, not being continuous or habitual, and it is required that it be perceived from the aspect of being contrary to the quality of the organ, then it is painful essentially in the sense of not being dependent on bad temperament, although its inflicting pain is through what follows from it of loss of the organ's form and its proper perfection. Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the cause of pain from disconnection of continuity (tafarruq al-ittiṣāl). The objector argues that if disconnection were the immediate cause of pain, then cutting a limb with a sharp tool should produce pain instantly, but it is actually felt only after a moment, once a bad temperament (sū’ al-mizāj) occurs. The author explains that the disconnection itself is not the complete cause; rather, pain arises from the perceived disconnection in a sensitive organ when the soul attends to it and the disconnection is perceived as contrary to the organ's proper quality. Pain may be mediated by loss of the organ's proper form. The author then refutes the claim that all disconnections should be equally painful: nutritional disconnection is habitual and beneficial, so it does not cause pain. Cutting quickly with attention causes immediate pain; without attention, pain is not felt. An example of distraction (e.g., absorbed in thought) supports this: one may not feel hunger or pain due to preoccupation.

Correction: Pain depends on the disconnection being perceived as contrary to the organ's well-being, with the soul's attention, and not being habitual or beneficial.

D) The presence of a bad temperament.

Key Takeaway
Pain from disconnection requires the disconnection to be perceived as contrary to the organ's well-being, with the soul's attention; it is not solely determined by the physical event.

Distinguishing Causes of Pain: Wound vs. Scorpion Sting
Objection and answer on pain causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a large wound may not be more painful than a scorpion sting, and how the objection is answered by distinguishing different causes of pain.

Core Idea
Pain can have different causes; not all pain is from disconnection.

This passage addresses a key objection against the theory that pain is solely caused by disconnection. The objector points to the counterexample of a scorpion sting, which is often more painful than a larger wound. The author's response reveals an important nuance: pain may arise from other factors, such as toxicity, which can overpower the effect of disconnection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: if disconnection causes pain, then a larger wound should be more painful than a scorpion sting because it involves more disconnection. Yet experience shows otherwise.
Notice the logical structure: cause → expected effect, but reality contradicts.
2
Identify the hidden assumption
The objection assumes that the scorpion sting's pain also comes from disconnection. That assumption is the key point the author will challenge.
Think about what must be true for the objection to hold.
3
Analyze the answer
The author denies that the sting's pain is due to disconnection. Instead, it may be due to a poisonous effect creating a bad temperament that is stronger than the wound's disconnection.
The author introduces a new causal factor: toxicity (summiyya).
4
Connect to the lesson's goal
This shows that pain is not a simple function of disconnection amount; different mechanisms can produce different intensities.
This refines the theory of pain without discarding disconnection entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : أنه لو كان سبباً لكانت الجراحة العظيمة أقوى إيلاماً من لسعة العقرب، لكون التفرق في الجراحة أكثر وجوابه أن ذلك : إنما يلزم لو كان ألم لسعة العقرب أيضاً لتفرق الاتصال وهو ليس بلازم لجواز أن يكون لما يحصل بواسطة السمية من سوء مزاج مختلف أقوى تأثيراً من الجراحة العظيمة
And among them: that if it were a cause, then a large wound would be more painful than a scorpion sting, because the disconnection in the wound is greater. And its answer: that this would only follow if the pain of the scorpion sting were also due to disconnection of continuity, but it is not necessary, because it is possible that the pain comes from what occurs through the poisonous effect of a bad temperament of a different kind, stronger in effect than the large wound.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer concerning the causal relationship between disconnection (tafarruq) and pain. The objector argues that if disconnection were the cause of pain, then a large wound (which involves more disconnection) should be more painful than a scorpion sting (which involves less disconnection). But in reality, a scorpion sting is often more painful. The author answers that the pain of a scorpion sting does not necessarily come from disconnection; rather, it may come from the poison's effect causing a distinct bad temperament (sū’ mizāj mukhtalif) that is stronger in its impact than the disconnection from the large wound. Thus, the objection fails because it assumes the scorpion sting's pain is also from disconnection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All pain is caused by disconnection of continuity.
Correction: Pain can also arise from other factors such as toxicity, which may produce stronger pain than disconnection.

Checkpoint

B) Because the scorpion sting's pain may be due to a poisonous effect causing a stronger bad temperament.
C) Because large wounds are less sensitive.
D) Because scorpion stings are always less painful.

Key Takeaway
The scorpion sting objection is answered by positing a different causal mechanism for its pain, showing that pain is not solely a function of the amount of disconnection.

Objection and Answer: Scorpion Sting vs Large Wound
Objection and answer about pain causation from scorpion sting vs large wound

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that a large wound should be more painful than a scorpion sting is answered by distinguishing causes of pain (tearing vs. poison-induced temperament change).

Core Idea
Pain comes from different causes; the scorpion sting's severity is due to poison, not tearing.

In this passage, the author addresses a common objection against the theory that pain is caused by tearing of continuity (tafarruq al-ittisāl). The objection points to the scorpion sting as a counterexample: it causes intense pain despite minimal tearing. The response explains that the pain from a scorpion sting arises from a different cause—the poison disrupts the temperament—thereby preserving the original theory's explanatory scope.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection claims that if pain is from tearing, a large wound (more tearing) should be more painful than a scorpion sting. Notice the structure: conditional premise, observation of reality, conclusion.
2
Identify the core distinction in the answer
The answer distinguishes two causes: tearing (for wounds) and poison-induced temperament change (for scorpion stings). The latter can be more intense, so the objection does not refute the theory.
3
Reflect on the broader lesson about causality
The discussion teaches that a single type of effect (pain) can have multiple causes, and an objection based on a difference in intensity only works if the cause is assumed to be the same.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Or falling into battle and preoccupation with an important worldly matter might not perceive the pain of hunger and thirst, and many harmful things and likewise pleasures. Among them: that if it were a cause, then the large wound would be more painful than the scorpion sting, because the separation in the wound is greater. And its answer: that it only follows if the pain of the scorpion sting were also due to tearing of continuity, which is not necessary, because it is possible that what occurs through the poison of a different bad temperament has a stronger effect than the large wound.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the causation of pain. The objection argues that if pain were caused solely by the tearing of continuity (tafarruq al-ittisāl), then a large wound (with more tearing) should be more painful than a scorpion sting, which is not the case (since scorpion stings are often more painful). The answer explains that the pain of a scorpion sting is not due to tearing but to the poison causing a severe change in temperament (sū' mizāj mukhtalif), which can be more intense. Thus, different causes produce different pain intensities, so the objection does not invalidate the theory that some pains are due to tearing.

D) Because the pain of the sting is imaginary

Key Takeaway

Refutation of the claim that composition requires atom contact is merely conceptual
The author refutes an objection about atom contact and composition of body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that contact between atoms is a real accident necessary for body composition, and how this refutes the objection that composition would entail division.

Core Idea
Contact is a real accident, not a mere concept; without it, body composition is impossible.

This passage addresses a key objection in atomistic physics: if atoms come together to form a body, do they need to be in contact? Some argued that contact is merely conceptual to avoid infinite divisibility. The author counters that this contradicts both reason and sense, and clarifies the position of those who affirm the antagonism of accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first sentence of the passage, which presents the objection that contact is merely conceptual to avoid division.
The objector claims that composition would require contact, but they consider contact conceptual to avoid tajazzi (division).
2
Identify the rebuttal
Notice the author's immediate response: this contradicts reason and sense, because composition of a body from atoms is inconceivable without contact.
The author labels the objector as contradicting both rational and empirical evidence.
3
Understand the clarification
Read the author's statement about those who affirm the antagonism of accidents: they do not consider contact conceptual, implying it is a real accident.
This establishes the position of the school that contact is a real property necessary for composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جوهر لأكثر من جوهر تفادياً عن لزوم التجزي، فقد كابر متقضى العقل بل الحس، فإن تأليف الجسم من الجواهر عند من يقول بها لا يتصور بدون ذلك . قلنا : القائلون بتضاد الأكوان لا يجعلون المماسة منها أمراً اعتبارياً .
An atom for more than one atom, avoiding the necessity of division, for he has contradicted the dictate of reason, rather sense, for the composition of a body from atoms, according to those who affirm them, cannot be imagined without that. We said: Those who affirm the antagonism of accidents do not consider contact among them to be something conceptual.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that if a body is composed of more than one atom (jawhar), it would entail division (tajazzi), so they deny contact (mumāssa) between atoms. The author rebuts that this contradicts reason and sense, because composition of a body from atoms is inconceivable without contact. Then the author states that those who affirm the antagonism of accidents (taḍād al-akwān) do not consider contact to be merely conceptual (i‘tibārī); rather, it is a real accident. This clarifies the position of those who hold that contact is a real property necessary for composition.

Key Terms
جوهر
تجزي
تأليف الجسم
مماسة
تضاد الأكوان
اعتباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Contact between atoms is merely a conceptual construct to avoid the problem of infinite division.
Correction: The author argues that contact is a real accident necessary for body composition, and those who affirm the antagonism of accidents do not consider it conceptual.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue is impossible without contact between atoms?
A) The existence of atoms
B) The composition of a body
C) The antagonism of accidents
D) The divisibility of atoms

Key Takeaway
Contact between atoms is a real accident, not a mere concept; composition of a body requires real contact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Do you truly see the physical world around you as composed of real, concrete interactions (like contact) that reflect Allah’s deliberate creation, or do you sometimes dismiss them as mere abstract ideas? What would change if you consistently affirmed their reality?
?
The objector in this lesson denied something obvious to avoid a problem. In what area of your faith or life are you most prone to denying clear truths to protect your comfort? Will you commit to seeking the truth, even if it requires you to change your position?

The Rational Possibility of Rarefaction and Condensation
Sanusi establishes that measure is an accident, allowing change between part and whole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues that rarefaction and condensation are rationally possible by treating measure as an accident.

Core Idea
Measure is an accident, not a fixed essence, so change is possible.

The author begins by challenging the common-sense assumption that each body has a fixed, inseparable measure. He argues that measure is an accident that can change, and this avoids the need to posit a primitive matter (hyle) for change. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author first states the possibility that bodies have flexible measures.
The Arabic uses technical terms like هيولى and عرض.
2
Identify the refutation
Find where the author refutes the Imam's view that no hyle is needed. Understand why the author prefers measure as an accident.
The Imam mentioned is likely al-Razi.
3
Understand the part-whole analogy
The author argues that if the body is simple, part and whole are equal in nature, so the part can acquire the whole's measure (rarefaction) and vice versa. Grasp this logical step.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في نفسه كما هو عند الحس، فإنه ربما يختص كل جسم بمقدار معين لا ينقل عنه، وبهذا يندفع ما ذكره الإمام من أنه لا حاجة في ذلك إلى إثبات الهيولى بل يأتي على رأي من يجعل المقدار زائداً على الجسم عرضاً قائماً به سواء كان هو بسيطاً أو مركباً من الهيولى والصورة لأن نسبته إلى جميع المقادير على السوية كالهيولى ولأنه إذا كان بسيطاً كان الجزء والكل متساويين في الطبيعة والحقيقة فجاز اتصاف كل منهما بمقدار الآخر ما لم يمنع مانع، وانتقال الجزء إلى مقدار الكل تخلخل وعكسه تكاثف. نعم لا بد في ذلك أن من يصير الجزء منفصلاً إذ مع كونه جزءاً يمتنع أن يكون على مقدار الكل ضرورة. وأما الاعتراض بأنه لو جاز ذلك لجاز أن تصير القطرة على مقدار البحر وبالعكس. فجوابه بعد تسليم استحالة ذلك أن انتقال الجسم عن مقداره يكون لا محالة بقاسر فجاز أن يكون للقسر حد معين لا يمكن تجاوزه، كما جاز على القول بالهيولى أن يكون لكل مادة خط من المقدار لا يتجاوزه. وبالجملة فالمقصود بيان إمكان التخلخل والتكاثف وهو لا ينافي الامتناع في بعض الصور لمانع على أن اشتراط الانفصال في إمكان انتقال الجزء إلى مقدار الكل محل نظر دقيق . وقد يستدل على الوقوع بأن الماء إذا انجمد يصغر مقداره وهو تكاثف والجمد إذا ذاب يعظم مقداره وهو تخلخل، وبأن القارورة إذا مصت خرج منها هواء كثير، فلو لم يتخلخل الباقي لزم الخلاء، وإذا نفخت فيها دخلهـا هـواء كثير، فلو لم يتكاثف لزم التداخل أعني اشتغال حيز واحد بجسمين وهو ضروري الاستحالة .
In itself as it is in sensation, it may be that every body is specified with a specific measure that it does not transfer from. And by this is refuted what the Imam mentioned, that there is no need in that to establish hyle, but rather it proceeds according to the opinion of one who makes the measure an accident subsisting in the body, whether it is simple or composed of hyle and form, because its relation to all measures is equal like the hyle, and because if it is simple, then the part and the whole are equal in nature and reality, so it is possible for each of them to be described with the measure of the other unless there is a preventer preventing, and the transfer of the part to the measure of the whole is rarefaction and its opposite is condensation. Indeed, it is necessary that the part become separate, for as long as it is a part, it is impossible for it to be on the measure of the whole necessarily. As for the objection that if that were possible, then it would be possible for a drop to become the size of the sea and vice versa. Its answer, after acknowledging the impossibility of that, is that the transfer of the body from its measure is inevitably due to a force, so it is possible that the force has a specific limit that cannot be exceeded, just as it is possible on the basis of hyle that every matter has a line of measure that it does not exceed. In summary, the purpose is to demonstrate the possibility of rarefaction and condensation, and that does not preclude impossibility in some cases due to a preventer, although the condition of separation in the possibility of the part transferring to the measure of the whole is a subtle point of view. And evidence for occurrence is that when water freezes its measure becomes smaller, which is condensation, and when ice melts its measure becomes bigger, which is rarefaction; and that when a bottle is sucked, much air comes out of it, so if the remaining air did not rarely, vacuum would follow, and when you blow into it, much air enters it, so if it did not condense, interpenetration would follow, i.e., one space occupied by two bodies, which is necessarily impossible.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the possibility and actual occurrence of rarefaction (takhakhul) and condensation (takathuf). The author first refutes the view that bodies have fixed quantities, discussing the concept of measure as an accident. He addresses the objection that if such change were possible, a drop could become as large as the sea, answering that any change requires a force with a limit. Then he provides two empirical evidences: water freezing (contraction) and melting (expansion), and the example of air in a bottle to show that without rarefaction and condensation, vacuum or interpenetration would occur, both impossible. The author concludes that the objective is to prove possibility, not universal actuality.

Key Terms
مقدار
هيولى
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodies have fixed quantities that cannot change.
Correction: Measure is an accident, so it can change unless prevented.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument for the possibility of rarefaction and condensation?
A) Bodies have fixed quantities
B) Measure is an accident that can change
C) Hyle is necessary for change
D) Part and whole are always different in nature

Key Takeaway
Rarefaction and condensation are rationally possible because measure is an accident, not a fixed essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your life, what 'measures' (status, possessions, abilities) do you treat as fixed essences? How can you remind yourself that they are accidents subject to Allah's will?
?
The author says that a part can take on the measure of the whole unless prevented. What prevents you from expanding in goodness or knowledge? What external preventers do you need to remove, and how can you rely on Allah to remove them?

Refuting the Objection of Extreme Change
Sanusi addresses and resolves the objection that rarefaction/condensation implies unlimited change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that if rarefaction and condensation were possible, a drop could become the size of the sea.

Core Idea
Change due to rarefaction/condensation is limited by the force causing it.

A powerful objection to the possibility of rarefaction and condensation is that it would allow a drop to expand to the size of the ocean. The author provides a nuanced answer, showing that such extreme change is not entailed because the force (qasir) has a limit. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the passage identifying the objection (a drop becoming the sea) and the author's response.
The objection is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand the role of force (qasir)
The author argues that any change in measure is caused by a force, and that force has a specific limit, preventing extreme changes.
This parallels the idea of a 'line of measure' in the hyle theory.
3
Note the subtle point about separation
The author mentions that the condition of separation of the part from the whole is a subtle point, indicating that the argument is not trivial.

Empirical Evidence for Rarefaction and Condensation
Sanusi provides two empirical proofs for the actual occurrence of rarefaction and condensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will learn the empirical evidence that proves the actual occurrence of rarefaction and condensation in the physical world.

Core Idea
Water freezing and melting, and air in a bottle, show rarefaction and condensation occur.

After establishing rational possibility, the author provides empirical evidence from everyday phenomena: water expands when freezing and contracts when melting, and air behavior in a bottle demonstrates rarefaction and condensation. These observations are used to argue that without these processes, impossible consequences like vacuum or interpenetration would follow. Let's see the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic, noting the two pieces of evidence: water freezing/melting and the bottle example.
The bottle example involves suction and blowing.
2
Understand the water example
When water freezes, its measure decreases (condensation); when ice melts, its measure increases (rarefaction). This is observable.
3
Analyze the bottle example
When you suck air from a bottle, the remaining air must rarely to avoid vacuum; when you blow in air, the air must condense to avoid interpenetration (two bodies in one space).
Vacuum and interpenetration are deemed impossible.
4
Connect to the overall purpose
The author concludes that the aim is to demonstrate possibility, not that all cases are actual.

Objection and Reply on Composition of Motion from Indivisible Parts
Refuting the claim that finite units can compose motion by being infinitely divisible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that motion could be composed of infinitely divisible units by insisting on the instantaneous nature of transitions.

Core Idea
Motion requires a continuous substrate, not indivisible parts.

This lesson examines a key objection to the argument against indivisible parts. The objector suggests each finite unit could be infinitely divisible, but the reply shows this ignores the instantaneous transition assumption. The author reaffirms that motion's extension must correspond to a continuum, not a discrete set of finite units.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the structure: an objection from an imagined opponent, then the author's reply introduced by 'قلنا'.
The objection is introduced by 'فإن قيل'.
2
Identify the key claim of the objection
The objector claims that each finite unit can be divided infinitely, so motion need not consist of indivisible parts. Ask yourself: what assumption does this objection attack?
The objection targets the idea that motion is composed of finite, indivisible steps.
3
Analyze the reply's structure
The reply first calls the objection 'غير مقيد' (not restricted), then clarifies the assumption of instantaneous transition (دفعى). It concludes that motion requires infinite divisibility (like in a continuum). Notice the examples: motion in water (single continuum) vs. earth to heaven (different bodies).
The phrase 'والحاصل' introduces the conclusion.
4
Understand the final refutation
The reply ends by refuting a potential misunderstanding: if distance allows motion because it is infinitely divisible, then quantity itself (being intrinsically divisible) is even more suitable. This reinforces that motion cannot be composed of indivisible parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : يجوز أن يكون كل واحد من تلك الآحاد المتناهية قابلاً لانقسامات غير متناهية، فلا يلزم تركب الحركة مما لا تقبل الانقسام . قلنا : هذا غير مقيد إذ التقدير أن الانتقال إلى كل من تلك الأحاد دفعى والحاصل أن امتناع تركب الحركة مما لا ينقسم يقتضي أن يكون امتدادها منطبقاً على أمر قابل لانقسامات غير متناهية على ما هو شأن لكم واحد كما في الحركة في الماء، أو الأجسام مختلفة كما في الحركة من الأرض إلى السماء لا على كم منفصل متناهي الآحاد سواء كان معروضه جوهراً، أو كماً متصلاً أو كيف أو غير ذلك، وبهذا يندفع ما يتوهم من أنه إذا جازت الحركة في المسافة لكونها معروضة لما يقبل الانقسام لا إلى نهاية، ففي الكم القابل لذلك بحسب ذاته أولى.
For it is said: It is possible that each one of those finite units is divisible into infinite divisions, so it does not necessitate that motion is composed of what does not accept division. We say: This is not restricted, for the assumption is that the transition to each of those units is instantaneous, and the conclusion is that the impossibility of motion being composed of what does not divide requires that its extension corresponds to something receptive to infinite divisions, such as is the case for every single unit (كم واحد) as in motion in water, or different bodies as in motion from earth to heaven, not a separate quantity with finite units, whether its substrate is substance, or a continuous quantity, or quality, or other than that. And by this is refuted what is imagined: that if motion is possible in distance because it is a substrate for what accepts division without end, then in quantity that is receptive to that by its essence it is even more so.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer pair about the composition of motion. The objector argues: It is possible that each of the finite units (the supposed indivisible parts of motion) is itself divisible into infinite parts. Therefore, motion does not have to be composed of indivisible parts. The reply begins 'قلنا' (We say). The reply first calls the objection 'غير مقيد' (not restricted), meaning the assumption is flawed: the assumption in the original proof was that the transition to each such unit is instantaneous (دفعى), i.e., there is no intermediate. If each unit were divisible into infinite parts, those parts would not be traversed instantaneously, contradicting the premise. The conclusion (الحاصل) is that the impossibility of motion being composed of indivisible parts requires that the extension of motion correspond to something that is infinitely divisible, like the continuum (كم متصل) in the case of a single magnitude (as in motion through water), or different bodies (as in motion from earth to heaven). It is not a separate quantity (كم منفصل) with finite units. The substrate can be substance, continuous quantity, quality, etc. This argument refutes a potential misunderstanding: if motion is possible in distance (which is a continuous quantity) because distance is a substrate for what is infinitely divisible, then it is even more so in quantity that is intrinsically divisible without end. In other words, the reply confirms that motion requires a continuum, not indivisible parts.

Key Terms
حركة
انقسام
كم متصل
كم منفصل
دفعى
امتداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think motion can be composed of indivisible parts if those parts are themselves divisible.
Correction: The reply clarifies that the original argument assumed instantaneous transition, so the divisibility of a unit would mean it is not traversed instantaneously, contradicting the premise.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the objection that finite units can be infinitely divisible?
A) Because infinite division of finite units leads to contradiction
B) Because the objection ignores that the transition to each unit is instantaneous
C) Because motion cannot occur in a continuous quantity
D) Because finite units cannot exist in reality

Key Takeaway
Motion cannot be composed of indivisible parts because its extension requires a continuum that is infinitely divisible.

?
If your actions were composed of discrete, isolated moments, each with no connection to the next, would you still be responsible for your overall character? How does the continuous nature of motion remind you that your deeds are created by Allah as a single, flowing reality?
?
Consider your spiritual journey. Do you treat it as a series of independent acts (prayers, charities) or as a continuous movement towards Allah requiring constant divine support? Which view is more aligned with the lesson's insistence on a continuum?
?
Reflect on a time you assumed your progress could be broken into small, self-sufficient steps. Did that lead you to neglect the ongoing need for Allah's sustenance? Will you commit today to seeing your entire life as an extension requiring His continuous creation?
