Motion is Not Established in Substance and Other Categories
Sanusi denies motion in said categories and gives a specific argument for substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the classical Sunni theologian denies motion in several categories, especially substance, and the argument based on substance's inability to receive intensity.

Core Idea
Substance changes instantaneously; it cannot undergo gradual motion.

This passage addresses a fundamental distinction in Islamic theology: motion is not a property of all categories. In particular, substance (جوهر) does not accept gradual change. The author argues that substance changes by instantaneous transitions between forms, not by gradual motion. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage listing the categories
Read from the beginning to understand which categories are denied motion. The author lists: substance, when (position), relation, possession, action, and passion.
2
Focus on the argument for substance
The author explains that substance does not admit intensity (اشتداد), so its occurrence is instantaneous. Understand the two possibilities if it did admit intensity.
This is the core proof.
3
Consider the objection from quality
The passage mentions that this is criticized by motion in quality. Note the author acknowledges this but does not fully answer here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ولا ثبت للحركة في باقي المقولات . يعني لا دليل على ثبوت الحركة في الجوهر والمتى والإضافة والملك وأن يفعل وأن ينفعل، بل ربما يقام الدليل على نفيها . أما الجوهر فلأنه بعد ثبوت الكون وتوارد الصور على المادة الواحدة، فالانتقال إلى كل منها دفعى، لأن الجوهر لا يقبل الاشتداد فلا يكون حدوثه على التدريج، وذلك لأنه لو قبل الاشتداد فإما أن يبقى في وسط الاشتداد نوع الجوهر الذي منه الانتقال فلا يكون التغير فيه بل في لوازمه، أو لا يبقى، فيكون ذلك انتفاء لا اشتداداً، وهذا منقوص بالحركة في الكيف. وقد يحتج بأن المتحرك لابد أن يكون موجوداً والمادة وحدها غير موجودة لما سيجيء من امتناع وجودها بدون الصورة، وتحقيقه أن الحركة في الصور إنما تكون بتعاقب الصور على المادة بحيث لا تبقى بلا صورة زماناً، وعدم الصورة توجب عدم المادة لكونها مقومة للمادة بخلاف الكيف، فإن عدمه لا يوجب عدم المحل . وجوابه : ما سيجيء من أن تقوم المادة إنما هو بصورة ما فعدم الصورة المعينة إنما يوجب عدمها لو لم يستعقب حدوث صورة أخرى وأما ما قيل من أن تغيرات الجواهر أعني الأجسام بصورها لا تقع في كل آن زمان، لأن الصور لا تشاد ولا تضعف بل تقع في آن تغيرات الجواهر وتغيراتها بكيفياتها وكمياتها وأيونها وأوضاعها تقع في زمان لأنها تشتد وتضعف ومما نية هو اعتبار المحل الواحد الثابت بالقياس إلى حال فيه غير قار تتبدل نوعيته إذا قيس ما يوجد فيه في آن ما إلى ما يوجد في آن آخر بحيث يكون ما يوجد في كل آن متوسطاً بين ما يوجد في الآنين المحيطين به، ويتجدد جميعها على ذلك المحل المتقوم دونها من حيث هو متوجه بتلك التجـدادت إلى غاية ما، ومعنى الضعف هو ذلك المعنى بعينه إلا أنه يؤخذ من حيث هو منصرف بها عن تلك الغاية، فالأخذ في الشدة والضعف هو المحل لا الحال المتجدد المتصرم، ولا شك أن مثل هذا الحال يكون عرضاً لتقوم المحل دون
He said: And motion is not established in the remaining categories. That is, there is no proof for the existence of motion in substance, when (position), relation, possession, to act, and to be acted upon; rather, a proof may be given for its negation. As for substance, because after the establishment of existence and the succession of forms upon one matter, the transition to each of them is instantaneous, because substance does not admit intensity (اشتداد), so its occurrence cannot be gradual. And that is because if it admitted intensity, then either the type of substance from which the transition occurs remains in the midst of intensity, so change would not occur in it but in its concomitants, or it does not remain, then that would be non-existence, not intensity. And this is criticized by motion in quality. And it may be argued that the moving entity must exist, and matter alone does not exist, because of what will come about the impossibility of its existence without form. And its verification is that motion in forms only occurs by the succession of forms upon matter such that it does not remain without form for a moment, and the absence of form necessitates the absence of matter because it is constitutive of matter, unlike quality for its absence does not necessitate the absence of the substrate. And its answer: What will come that the subsistence of matter is only by some form, so the absence of a particular form only necessitates its absence if it is not followed by the occurrence of another form. And as for what was said that the changes of substances, i.e., bodies with their forms, do not occur in every moment of time, because forms do not intensify nor weaken, but rather occur in an instant, and the changes of substances and their changes by their qualities, quantities, positions, and postures occur in time because they intensify and weaken. And the meaning of intensity (شدة) is the consideration of the one fixed substrate with respect to a non-stable state in it the type of which changes when what is found in it at one moment is compared to what is found at another moment, such that what is found in each moment is intermediate between what is found in the two surrounding moments, and all of them are renewed upon that substrate which is subsistent without them, insofar as it is directed by those renewals towards a certain end. And the meaning of weakness (ضعف) is that same meaning except that it is taken insofar as it is turning away from that end. So the subject of intensity and weakness is the substrate, not the renewed transitory state. And there is no doubt that such a state is an accident to the subsistence of the substrate without
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a proof that motion is not established in several categories: substance (جوهر), when (متى), relation (إضافة), possession (ملك), to act (أن يفعل), and to be acted upon (أن ينفعل). The author explains that for substance, since substance does not admit intensity (اشتداد), its occurrence cannot be gradual; the transition to each form is instantaneous. He then examines a counterargument: if substance admitted intensity, either the original type persists (so change is in its concomitants, not itself) or it ceases (which is annihilation, not intensity). This is then criticized by analogy with motion in quality (which does admit intensity). Next, an objection is raised: the moving entity must exist, but matter alone does not exist without form; so motion in forms involves succession of forms, and absence of form would annihilate matter. The answer is that matter subsists by some form; the absence of a particular form only annihilates if not immediately replaced. Finally, the passage defines intensity (شدة) and weakness (ضعف) in terms of a fixed substrate (محل واحد ثابت) undergoing gradual change towards a goal (غاية). The substrate remains, while the accident (الحال) is non-stable and renewed. This shows that motion is a gradual accident in time, not applicable to substance which changes instantly.

Key Terms
حركة
جوهر
متى
إضافة
ملك
اشتداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that substance can change gradually like accidents.
Correction: The author argues substance changes instantaneously because it does not accept intensity.

Checkpoint
Why does the author deny motion in substance?
A) Because substance is not a category of accidents
B) Because substance does not admit intensity
C) Because substance is not located in space
D) Because substance is eternal

Key Takeaway
Substance cannot undergo gradual motion; its transitions between forms are instantaneous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that certain bad habits or spiritual states require gradual effort to change. But this lesson teaches that Allah can transform your very essence in an instant. What is one area of your life where you have been relying on slow self-improvement instead of crying out to Allah for immediate change? Will you make that dua today?
?
The argument in the passage shows that substance cannot gradually become something else; it must be created anew. Are there aspects of your identity—your sense of self, your pride, your attachments—that you think you can gradually refine, but in reality need to be completely replaced by Allah's action? What would it mean for you to surrender that to Him?

The Nature of Gradual Change: Intensity, Weakness, and the Substrate
Sanusi answers an objection and defines intensity/weakness as gradual change in accidents on a fixed substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how motion occurs gradually in accidents through intensity and weakness, and the role of the substrate in such change.

Core Idea
Intensity and weakness are changes in the accident, not in the substrate.

In this continuation, the author addresses an objection about motion in forms and then defines intensity and weakness. He clarifies how gradual change occurs in accidents by focusing on the fixed substrate and the renewed states. Let's explore this deeper.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
The objection is that motion in forms requires matter to exist without form, but matter cannot exist without form. The answer is that matter subsists by some form, and absence of a particular form is not permanent.
2
Understand the definition of intensity
The author defines intensity (شدة) as considering a fixed substrate with a non-stable state that changes gradually towards a goal. Weakness is the opposite direction.
3
Identify the subject of change
The author concludes that the subject of intensity and weakness is the substrate, not the transient accident itself.

The Argument on Intensity and the Response Regarding Substantial Change
Analyzes intensity in change with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why intensity (اشتداد) applies only to accidental change over time, not to substantial change which occurs in an instant.

Core Idea
Intensity is a change in the substrate's accidents, not its substance.

This passage tackles a key question in kalam: can a substance itself undergo intensity? The author presents an argument that if intensity occurred in the substance, it would either be accidental change or annihilation. He then clarifies that true intensity belongs to accidental categories like quality, which occur over time, while substantial changes happen in an instant. Let's read the source to grasp this crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the full translation. Let the author's own argument flow through you.
Pay attention to the conditional structure at the beginning.
2
Identify the conditional argument
The passage starts with 'if the substance accepted intensity'. Find the two possibilities and their implications. Understand why intensity cannot apply to the substance itself.
3
Understand the objection and its answer
Next, note the objection about matter and form. The answer clarifies that matter requires form, but absence of a specific form does not annihilate matter if another form immediately arises. This supports the possibility of substantial change in an instant.
4
Grasp the distinction between substantial and accidental change
Finally, see how the author distinguishes between changes of substances (instant, no intensity) and changes of accidents (time-based, with intensity). Understand the definitions of intensity and weakness.
This is the main doctrinal point.

And that is because if [the substance] accepted intensity, then either the type of substance from which the transition originates remains in the midst of intensity, so change is not in it but in its accidents, or it does not remain, and that would be an annihilation, not an intensity. And this is criticized by motion in quality. And it may be argued that the moving thing must exist, and matter alone does not exist because of what will come of the impossibility of its existence without form. Its verification is that motion in forms occurs only by the succession of forms upon matter such that it does not remain without form for a time, and the absence of form necessitates the non-existence of matter because form constitutes matter, unlike quality, for its absence does not necessitate the non-existence of the substrate. Intensity. Its answer: what will come that the constitution of matter is only by some form, so the absence of a specific form only necessitates its non-existence if it is not followed by the occurrence of another form. And as for what was said that changes of substances, i.e., bodies with their forms, do not occur in every instant of time, because forms do not intensify nor weaken but occur in the instant of changes of substances, and their changes by their qualities, quantities, positions, and postures occur in time because they intensify and weaken. And its meaning is the consideration of the one fixed substrate in relation to a non-stable state whose type changes when what exists in an instant [measured] relative to what exists in another instant, such that what exists in each instant is intermediate between what exists in the two instants surrounding it, and all of them are renewed upon that substrate which is constituted without them, inasmuch as it is directed by those renewals towards a goal. And the meaning of weakness is that same meaning except that it is taken inasmuch as it is turned away by them from that goal. So the occurrence of intensity and weakness applies to the substrate, not to the transient renewed state. And there is no doubt that such a state is an accident for the constitution of the substrate without each one of those identities.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage presents an argument and response regarding the nature of intensity (اشتداد) in change. It begins with a conditional: if a substance undergoes intensity, either the type of substance remains (so change is in its accidents, not substance) or it does not remain (so it is annihilation, not intensity). This is criticized by motion in quality, which can be intensive. Then an objection is raised: the moving thing must exist; matter alone does not exist without form because form constitutes matter. Motion in forms involves succession of forms on matter with no time gap; absence of form annihilates matter, unlike quality. The answer clarifies that matter is constituted by 'some form', so the absence of a specific form only causes non-existence if another form does not immediately arise. The passage then distinguishes between changes of substances (bodies with forms) which occur in an instant (not in time, because forms do not intensify), and changes of qualities, quantities, etc., which occur in time because they intensify and weaken. It defines intensity as the consideration of a fixed substrate in relation to a non-stable state that changes in type when comparing instants; the substrate undergoes renewal toward a goal. Weakness is the same but away from the goal. Thus intensity/weakness belong to the substrate, not the transient state, which is an accident. This guards against confusing substantial change with accidental intensive change.

Correction: The passage shows that intensity applies only to accidents (qualities, etc.) and refers to the substrate's relation to successive states, not the substance's essence.

C) Because matter cannot receive forms in time.
D) Because the substrate does not exist.

Key Takeaway
Intensity and weakness are characteristics of accidental change in time, while substantial change occurs in an instant without intensity.

?

How does this lesson's distinction between substantial and accidental change challenge you to see your fixed, divinely-constituted core that remains? Name one present change that is merely accidental, and commit to letting your substance remain firm while the accidents shift, turning toward your ultimate goal (إلى غاية ما).
?
The lesson defines intensity and weakness as directed toward or away from a goal. What is your ultimate goal (غاية)? Are your current 'intensities'—your passionate pursuits—and your 'weaknesses'—your declines—moving you toward that goal or away? Confess honestly: is there an area where you are 'turning away' (منصرف بها عن تلك الغاية)? What concrete step will you take today to redirect that movement back toward your purpose?
?
Form constitutes matter; the absence of a specific form would annihilate the matter if not immediately succeeded by another form. Think of a moment when you lost a defining 'form'—a role, a belief, a relationship—and felt emptiness. Did you trust that another form would arise, or did you despair? How does this theological truth about form and matter inform your trust in God's constant provision of what sustains you? Will you, right now, surrender your need for a particular form and trust the One who never leaves a substrate empty?

The Category of 'When' and Ibn Sina's Views
Examines motion in the category of 'when' and the relative nature of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the category 'when' (متى) is considered a relative and non-independent category that does not undergo motion itself, based on Ibn Sina's arguments and later scholarly endorsement.

Core Idea
'When' is a relation to time, not a substance that can move.

This lesson explores Ibn Sina's analysis of the category 'when' (متى) as presented in his works al-Najat and al-Shifa. Ibn Sina argues that motion cannot occur in 'when' itself because that would lead to an infinite regress; instead, transition in 'when' is instantaneous and 'when' is analogous to relation (الإضافة), a non-independent nature. The passage concludes with the Imam's endorsement and an extension to possession (الملك). Understanding this helps clarify the nature of time in Islamic metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the flow from form/identity to Ibn Sina's two works and the analogy with relation.
Pay attention to the objection about matter's identity.
2
Identify the key arguments
Identify Ibn Sina's argument in al-Najat (infinite regress) and in al-Shifa (instantaneous transition and the relation analogy). Understand why 'when' cannot be a locus of motion.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider the Imam's statement that 'when' is a relation to time and must follow its substrate. Reflect on how this view affects the understanding of time as a concomitant.
Connect to the broader discussion of categories and change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحال الذي تتبدل هوية المحل المتقوم بتبدله وهي الصورة، فلا يتصور فيها اشتداد ولا ضعف لامتناع تبدلها على شيء واحد متقوم يكون هو هو في الحالين، فجمع بين الوجهين مع تفصيل وتحقيق ويرد علية ما سبق مع أنا لا نسلم تبدل هوية المادة بتبدل الصورة. وقد صرح ابن سينا بأن الوحدة الشخصية للمادة مستحفظة بالوحدة النوعية للصورة لا بالوحدة الشخصية، وأما المتى فذكر في النجاة أنه لا بد للحركة من متى . فلو وقعت حركة في المتى لكان للمتى متى وهو باطل، وذكر في الشفاء أن الانتقال فيه دفعى لأن الانتقال من سنة إلى سنة ومن شهر إلى شهر يكون دفعة ثم قال ويشبه أن يكون حالة كحال الإضافة في أن الانتقال لا يكون فيه، بل يكون الانتقال الأول في كم أو كيف ويكون الزمان لازماً لذلك التغير فيفرض بسببه فيه التبدل كما أن الإضافة طبيعة غير مستقلة، بل تابعة لغيرها، فإن كان المتبوع قابلاً للأشد والأنقص. فكذا الإضافة إذ لو بقيت غير متغيرة عند تغير متبوعها لزم استقلالها .، قال الإمام وهذا هو الحق لأن متى نسبة إلى الزمان والنسبة طبيعة غير مستقلة فهي تابعة لمعروضها في التبدل والاستقرار وكذا الملك لأنها مقولة نسبية. وقيل لأنها توجد دفعة
As for the state in which the identity of the constituted substrate changes by its change, which is form, then intensity and weakness are not conceivable in it because of the impossibility of its change upon one constituted thing that is the same in both states. Thus he combined both aspects with detail and verification, and what preceded is objected to it, with the statement that we do not accept the change of the identity of matter by the change of form. Ibn Sina explicitly stated that the personal unity of matter is preserved by the specific unity of form, not by personal unity. As for 'when' (متى), he mentioned in al-Najat that motion necessarily requires 'when'. So if motion occurred in 'when', then 'when' would have a 'when', which is false. And he mentioned in al-Shifa that transition in it is instantaneous because transition from year to year and from month to month occurs instantaneously. Then he said: it resembles the case of relation (الإضافة) in that transition does not occur in it, rather the primary transition is in quantity or quality, and time is a necessary concomitant of that change, so by it [time] change is posited in it, just as relation is a non-independent nature, rather dependent on something else. If the subject is susceptible to more and less, then so too is the relation, because if it remained unchanged when its subject changes, its independence would be required. The Imam said: this is the truth, because 'when' is a relation to time, and relation is a non-independent nature, so it follows its substrate in change and stability, and likewise possession (الملك) because it is a relative category. And it was said: because they occur instantaneously.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of change, focusing on the category of 'when' (متى) and Ibn Sina's views. First, it contrasts the state where the substrate's identity changes (form) – here intensity and weakness are inconceivable because the substrate changes entirely. The author combines both aspects with detail and notes an objection: he does not accept that matter's identity changes with form. Ibn Sina is cited: the personal unity of matter is preserved by the specific unity of form, not by personal unity. For 'when', Ibn Sina in al-Najat says motion requires 'when', but a motion in 'when' would lead to an infinite regress (a 'when' of 'when'), which is false. In al-Shifa, he says transition in 'when' is instantaneous (e.g., from year to year occurs in an instant). He compares it to relation (الإضافة): transition does not occur in it; rather, primary transition is in quantity or quality, and time is a necessary concomitant. Change in time is posited by that change, just as relation depends on something else; if the subject changes, the relation changes accordingly, otherwise relation would be independent. The Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) endorses this view: 'when' is a relation to time, a non-independent nature, so it follows its substrate in change and stability. The same applies to possession (الملك) as a relative category. Some say they occur instantaneously. The passage thus argues that 'when' does not itself undergo motion; motion is in other categories, and time is a concomitant.

Correction: The passage shows that 'when' is a relative category that does not undergo motion; it changes only as a concomitant of changes in other categories (quantity or quality). Time is a necessary concomitant, not an independent substance.

Checkpoint

C) It does not occur at all because 'when' is static.
D) Through intensity and weakness.

Key Takeaway
The category 'when' (متى) is a relative notion that does not undergo motion itself; motion is in other categories, and time is a necessary concomitant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that time itself is moving, and that you are just a passenger? This lesson argues that 'when' is merely a relation—it changes only because you change. Will you stop blaming time for your stagnation and instead examine what actually moves in your character (your actions, your qualities)?
?
Ibn Sina's argument uses an infinite regress to show the absurdity of motion in 'when'. In your own life, do you ever chase endless 'when's—waiting for the right time—while neglecting the real change that should happen in your heart? What would it take for you to focus on the 'what' and 'how' of your deeds rather than the 'when'?
?
The passage says that 'when' follows its substrate in change and stability. If your spiritual state is stagnant, your 'when' feels stuck. By contrast, if you change your actions and intentions (the substrate), your relation to time shifts. Will you today identify one concrete change in your worship or character, and commit to it regardless of the 'when'?

Refutation of Motion in Action and Passion
Dilemma refuting motion in action and passion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why motion cannot occur in the categories of action (أن يفعل) and passion (أن ينفعل) according to the kalam argument presented.

Core Idea
Motion in action and passion is impossible: it requires simultaneous opposites or a discontinuity.

This passage examines the claim that motion exists in the categories of action and passion. The author presents a logical argument showing that such motion leads to contradictions or breaks in continuity, thereby refuting the claim. We will follow the reasoning step by step, noting where the text is incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Understand the categories
Action (أن يفعل) and passion (أن ينفعل) are two of Aristotle's categories. Some philosophers claimed motion occurs in them. The author denies this.
These categories involve active and passive qualities like heating and cooling.
2
Follow the dilemma
The author gives a hypothetical transition from cooling to heating. If cooling persists during the transition, then cold and heat would coexist, which is impossible. If cooling ceases first, then there is a time of rest, so no continuous transition – hence no motion.
The argument uses the principle of non-contradiction and the definition of motion as continuous change.
3
Note the incompleteness
The passage cuts off mid-sentence when addressing a counter-argument. Be aware that the full refutation may continue on the next page.
This lesson covers only the core dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال وإما أن يفعل وأن ينفعل ، فأثبت بعضهم فيهما الحركة والحق بطلانه (أما أن يفعل ) فلأن الشيء إذا انتقل من التبرد إلى التسخن مثلاً ، فإن كان التربد باقياً لزم التوجيه إلى الضدين. أعني البرودة والسحونة في زمان واحد، وإن لم يكن باقياً، بل إنمـا وجـد التسخن بعد وقوف التبرد وبينهما زمان سكون لا محالة فليس هناك انتقال من التبرد إلى التسخن على الاستمرار وما يقال من أن الشيء قد ينسلخ عن
Then he said: as for 'to act' (أن يفعل) and 'to be acted upon' (أن ينفعل), some affirmed motion in them, but the truth is its falsehood. (As for 'to act') because if a thing transitions from cooling to heating, for example, then if the cooling remains, it necessitates direction to two opposites, namely coldness and heat, in one time. And if it does not remain, but rather heating occurs after the cessation of cooling, and between them there is necessarily a time of rest, then there is no continuous transition from cooling to heating. And what is said that a thing may shed [itself]...
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage addresses the categories of action (أن يفعل) and passion (أن ينفعل) and examines whether motion occurs in them. The author states that some have affirmed motion in these categories, but the truth is its falsehood. The argument for action is given: if a thing transitions from cooling to heating, either the cooling remains (which would require simultaneous opposites) or it does not remain (so heating occurs after cooling ceases, with a time of rest in between, meaning no continuous transition). The passage begins to address a counter-argument ('what is said that a thing may shed...') but the text cuts off at the page end. The argument aims to show that motion in action/passion is impossible because it would either involve co-presence of opposites or a discontinuity, not true continuous motion.

The Identity of Matter and Form
Discussion on the impossibility of gradual change in form due to its role in constituting substrate identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why form cannot undergo intensification or weakness while preserving the identity of the substrate, and how Ibn Sina's distinction between personal and specific unity resolves the issue.

Core Idea
Form defines identity; matter persists through specific unity, not personal.

In this passage, the author examines the relationship between matter and form, addressing a key ontological puzzle: can a substrate remain identical while its form changes? The discussion draws on Ibn Sina's distinction between personal and specific unity to explain how matter preserves identity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on form and identity
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the statement that intensification and weakness are inconceivable in form due to the impossibility of change on an identical substrate.
2
Understand the refutation and Ibn Sina's view
Notice how the author refutes a previous view and then cites Ibn Sina to show that matter's personal unity is preserved through the form's specific unity.
This distinguishes personal identity from specific identity.
3
Consider the implications
Think about how this argument applies to real-world cases: a living organism's form changes over time but its matter remains the same in a specific sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل واحدة من تلك الهويات. وأما الحال الذي تتبدل هوية المحل المتقوم بتبدله وهي الصورة، فلا يتصور فيها اشتداد ولا ضعف لامتناع تبدلها على شيء واحد متقوم يكون هو هو في الحالين، فجمع بين الوجهين مع تفصيل وتحقيق ويرد عليه ما سبق مع أنا لا نسلم تبدل هوية المادة بتبدل الصورة. وقد صرح ابن سينا بأن الوحدة الشخصية للمادة مستحفظة بالوحدة النوعية للصورة لا بالوحدة الشخصية، وأما المتى فذكر في النجاة أنه لا بد للحركة من متى. فلو وقعت حركة في المتى لكان للمتى متى وهو باطل، وذكر في الشفاء أن الانتقال فيه دفعى لأن الانتقال من سنة إلى سنة ومن شهر إلى شهر يكون دفعة ثم قال ويشبه أن يكون حالة كحال الإضافة في أن الانتقال لا يكون فيه، بل يكون الانتقال الأول في كم أو كيف ويكون الزمان لازماً لذلك التغير فيفرض بسببه فيه التبدل كما أن الإضافة طبيعة غير مستقلة، بل تابعة لغيرها، فإن كان المتبوع قابلاً للأشد والأنقص. فكذا الإضافة إذ لو بقيت غير متغيرة عند تغير متبوعها لزم استقلالها. قال الإمام وهذا هو الحق لأن متى نسبة إلى الزمان والنسبة طبيعة غير مستقلة فهي تابعة لمعروضها في التبدل والاستقرار وكذا الملك لأنها مقولة نسبية. وقيل لأنها توجد دفعة
Each one of those identities. As for the state in which the identity of the substrate that is constituted by it changes, which is the form, then intensification and weakness cannot be conceived in it, because of the impossibility of its change upon one substrate that remains the same in both states. So he combined the two aspects with detail and investigation, and what preceded is refuted, while we do not concede that the identity of matter changes with the change of form. And Ibn Sina stated that the personal unity of matter is preserved by the specific unity of form, not by the personal unity. As for 'when', he mentioned in al-Najat that motion must have a 'when'. So if motion occurred in 'when', then 'when' would have a 'when', which is invalid. And he mentioned in al-Shifa' that transition in it is instantaneous, because the transition from year to year and from month to month is instantaneous. Then he said: And it resembles the case of relation (al-idafa) in that there is no transition in it, but rather the primary transition is in quantity or quality, and time is incidental to that change, so due to it change is supposed in it, just as relation is a non-independent nature, rather dependent on something else. So if the dependent is susceptible to intensification and diminution, then likewise relation, for if it remained unchanged when its subject changed, its independence would be necessitated. The Imam said: And this is the truth, because 'when' (al-mata) is a relation to time, and relation is a non-independent nature, so it follows its subject in change and stability. Likewise 'possession' (al-milk) because it is a relative category. And it was said that it exists instantaneously.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage discusses the identity of matter and form, and the nature of the category 'mata' (when). The author begins by noting that the identity of the substrate (al-mahall) that is constituted by the form cannot undergo intensification or weakening because the form changes the substrate's identity; thus it cannot be the same substrate in two states. He then references a previous view that is refuted, and states that we do not accept that matter's identity changes with the change of form. Ibn Sina is cited to support that the personal unity of matter is preserved by the specific unity of form, not by personal unity. Then the discussion turns to 'mata' (when). Ibn Sina in al-Najat asserts that motion requires a 'when', but if motion were in 'when' itself, there would be an infinite regress. In al-Shifa', he says transition in 'when' is instantaneous, analogous to relation (al-idafa). Just as relation is a non-independent nature dependent on its subject, change in relation follows change in its subject. The Imam (likely al-Razi) affirms this, stating that 'mata' is a relation to time, thus dependent on its subject. Similarly, 'possession' (al-milk) is a relative category. The passage ends with a remark that 'it was said that it exists instantaneously'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that form can gradually change while the substrate remains numerically identical.
Correction: The passage shows that if the form changes, the substrate's identity changes, so gradual change is impossible. Ibn Sina's solution shows that identity is preserved through specific unity, not individual form.

Checkpoint
Why is intensification and weakness not conceivable in the form?
A) Because the form changes the identity of the substrate, so it cannot remain the same in two states.
B) Because the form is a relative accident like al-idafa.
C) Because motion in the form is instantaneous.
D) Because the form is the matter itself.

Key Takeaway
The identity of matter is preserved through the specific unity of form, allowing for change without compromising the substrate's underlying identity.

Where have you been clinging to a specific form (a job, a relationship, an appearance) instead of anchoring your identity in what is essential and unchanging about you, like your core faith and values? ?

If your physical form changes, what is it that remains constant? Are you actively nurturing that constant part of you, or are you distracted by temporary forms? What would it mean to truly trust that your identity in Allah's sight is not based on appearances?

Ibn Sina's insight separates personal unity from specific unity. In your own life, have you mistaken a personal characteristic (like a skill or a social role) for your essential nature? The Nature of 'Mata' (When) as a Relative Accident
Examination of the nature of 'mata' as a relational accident dependent on other changes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the category of 'mata' (when) is a non-independent relation to time, analogous to other relative categories like possession.

Core Idea
'Mata' is nothing but a relation to time, dependent on change in other categories.

The second part of the passage turns to the category of 'when' (al-mata). Drawing on Ibn Sina's arguments from al-Najat and al-Shifa', the author shows that time is not a substrate for motion but a relation that follows other changes. The Imam (al-Razi) affirms that 'mata' is a relative accident, dependent on its subject.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument from al-Najat
Read the first part: motion must have a 'when', but if motion were in 'when', there would be an infinite regress. Understand the problem.
2
Examine the analogy with relation
In al-Shifa', Ibn Sina compares 'when' to relation (al-idafa): just as relation is non-independent and follows its subject, 'when' follows changes in other categories like quantity or quality.
3
Al-Razi's conclusion
Read the final part where the Imam (al-Razi) endorses this view and extends it to 'possession' (al-milk), concluding that 'when' is a relative category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل واحدة من تلك الهويات. وأما الحال الذي تتبدل هوية المحل المتقوم بتبدله وهي الصورة، فلا يتصور فيها اشتداد ولا ضعف لامتناع تبدلها على شيء واحد متقوم يكون هو هو في الحالين، فجمع بين الوجهين مع تفصيل وتحقيق ويرد عليه ما سبق مع أنا لا نسلم تبدل هوية المادة بتبدل الصورة. وقد صرح ابن سينا بأن الوحدة الشخصية للمادة مستحفظة بالوحدة النوعية للصورة لا بالوحدة الشخصية، وأما المتى فذكر في النجاة أنه لا بد للحركة من متى. فلو وقعت حركة في المتى لكان للمتى متى وهو باطل، وذكر في الشفاء أن الانتقال فيه دفعى لأن الانتقال من سنة إلى سنة ومن شهر إلى شهر يكون دفعة ثم قال ويشبه أن يكون حالة كحال الإضافة في أن الانتقال لا يكون فيه، بل يكون الانتقال الأول في كم أو كيف ويكون الزمان لازماً لذلك التغير فيفرض بسببه فيه التبدل كما أن الإضافة طبيعة غير مستقلة، بل تابعة لغيرها، فإن كان المتبوع قابلاً للأشد والأنقص. فكذا الإضافة إذ لو بقيت غير متغيرة عند تغير متبوعها لزم استقلالها. قال الإمام وهذا هو الحق لأن متى نسبة إلى الزمان والنسبة طبيعة غير مستقلة فهي تابعة لمعروضها في التبدل والاستقرار وكذا الملك لأنها مقولة نسبية. وقيل لأنها توجد دفعة
Each one of those identities. As for the state in which the identity of the substrate that is constituted by it changes, which is the form, then intensification and weakness cannot be conceived in it, because of the impossibility of its change upon one substrate that remains the same in both states. So he combined the two aspects with detail and investigation, and what preceded is refuted, while we do not concede that the identity of matter changes with the change of form. And Ibn Sina stated that the personal unity of matter is preserved by the specific unity of form, not by the personal unity. As for 'when', he mentioned in al-Najat that motion must have a 'when'. So if motion occurred in 'when', then 'when' would have a 'when', which is invalid. And he mentioned in al-Shifa' that transition in it is instantaneous, because the transition from year to year and from month to month is instantaneous. Then he said: And it resembles the case of relation (al-idafa) in that there is no transition in it, but rather the primary transition is in quantity or quality, and time is incidental to that change, so due to it change is supposed in it, just as relation is a non-independent nature, rather dependent on something else. So if the dependent is susceptible to intensification and diminution, then likewise relation, for if it remained unchanged when its subject changed, its independence would be necessitated. The Imam said: And this is the truth, because 'when' (al-mata) is a relation to time, and relation is a non-independent nature, so it follows its subject in change and stability. Likewise 'possession' (al-milk) because it is a relative category. And it was said that it exists instantaneously.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage discusses the identity of matter and form, and the nature of the category 'mata' (when). The author begins by noting that the identity of the substrate (al-mahall) that is constituted by the form cannot undergo intensification or weakening because the form changes the substrate's identity; thus it cannot be the same substrate in two states. He then references a previous view that is refuted, and states that we do not accept that matter's identity changes with the change of form. Ibn Sina is cited to support that the personal unity of matter is preserved by the specific unity of form, not by personal unity. Then the discussion turns to 'mata' (when). Ibn Sina in al-Najat asserts that motion requires a 'when', but if motion were in 'when' itself, there would be an infinite regress. In al-Shifa', he says transition in 'when' is instantaneous, analogous to relation (al-idafa). Just as relation is a non-independent nature dependent on its subject, change in relation follows change in its subject. The Imam (likely al-Razi) affirms this, stating that 'mata' is a relation to time, thus dependent on its subject. Similarly, 'possession' (al-milk) is a relative category. The passage ends with a remark that 'it was said that it exists instantaneously'.

Correction: The passage shows that 'when' is a dependent relation to time; changes in 'when' are merely reflections of changes in other categories.

A) Because motion is instantaneous.
B) Because 'when' is a relation to time.
C) Because it would lead to an infinite regress of 'when's.
D) Because 'when' does not exist.

Key Takeaway
'When' (mata) is not an independent category but a relation to time that depends on changes in other categories, similar to other relative accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting Gradual Change in Action and Passion
Proof that action and passion categories lack gradual change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the categories of 'to act' and 'to be acted upon' do not admit gradual change, and how any apparent gradual change actually occurs in accessories.

Core Idea
Action and passion do not change gradually; their prerequisites do.

In this passage, the author takes on a subtle metaphysical mistake: the idea that the categories of action and passion can change gradually. Using the example of cooling to heating, he demonstrates that continuous change is impossible in the act itself. Any gradual change is actually in the power, instrument, or recipient. Let's trace the argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim being refuted
Some thinkers affirmed motion (gradual change) in the categories of 'to act' and 'to be acted upon'. The author states that the truth is its falsity. Recognize that this is the thesis to be disproven.
This sets up the whole argument.
2
Follow the proof for 'to act'
The author uses the example of a thing transitioning from cold to heat. Analyze the two possibilities: if cold remained, opposites would coexist; if cold ceased, there is a time of rest, so no continuous transition.
This shows the impossibility of gradual change in the act itself.
3
Examine the objection and its answer
An objection claims gradual shedding of actuality. The author responds by distinguishing that the gradual change is not in the act itself but in the power, resolve, or instrument. This is a key nuance.
The objection tries to save gradual change, but the answer shifts the locus of change.
4
Apply the same logic to 'to be acted upon'
The same reasoning applies: any gradual change is actually in the recipient (al-qabil), not in the passion itself. The author cites al-Mawaqif to confirm that these categories follow motion only in their accessories.
This completes the refutation for both categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال وإما أن يفعل وأن ينفعل، فأثبت بعضهم فيهما الحركة والحق بطلانه (أما أن يفعل) فلأن الشيء إذا انتقل من التبرد إلى التسخن مثلاً، فإن كان التربد باقياً لزم التوجيه إلى الضدين. أعني البرودة والسحونة في زمان واحد، وإن لم يكن باقياً، بل إنما وجد التسخن بعد وقوف التبرد وبينهما زمان سكون لا محالة فليس هناك انتقال من التبرد إلى التسخن على الاستمرار وما يقال من أن الشيء قد ينسلخ عن اتصافه بالفعل يسيراً يسيراً لا من جهة ينقص قبول الموضوع لتمام ذلك الفعل، بل من جهة هيئته فذلك عائد إلى أن فتور القوة، أو انفساخ العزيمة، أو كلال الآلة تكون يسيراً يسيراً أو بتبعية ذلك يحصل التبدل في الفاعلية، فما توهم من التغير التدريجي في أن يفعل نفسه إنما هو فيما يتم به الفعل، كما إذا توهم في أن ينفعل بناء على تحققه فيما يتم به الانفعال كالقابل وهذا ما قال في المواقف الحق أنهما تبع الحركة، أما في القوة إرادية كانت أو طبيعية، أو في الآلة، وأما في القابل وأتى في القابل بلفظ أما دون أو تنبيهاً على ما ذكرنا.
Then he said: As for 'to act' and 'to be acted upon', some affirmed motion in them, but the truth is its falsity. As for 'to act', because when a thing transitions from being cold to being hot, for example, if the coldness remained, it would necessitate facing both opposites, namely coldness and heat, in one time. And if it did not remain, but rather heat came after the cessation of coldness, there is necessarily a time of rest between them, so there is no continuous transition from cold to heat. And what is said that a thing may gradually shed its attribute of actuality, not from the aspect that the reception of the subject decreases for that actuality, but from the aspect of its state, that returns to the weakness of the power, or the dissolution of resolve, or the fatigue of the instrument occurring gradually, or by consequence of that the change occurs in the agentness (al-fa‘iliyya). So what is imagined of gradual change in 'to act' itself is only in that by which the act is completed, just as if he imagines in 'to be acted upon' based on its realization in that by which the passion is completed, like the recipient. And this is what was said in al-Mawaqif: the truth is that they follow motion, either in the power, whether voluntary or natural, or in the instrument, and as for the recipient, he used the word 'amma' (as for) instead of 'aw' (or), as a reminder of what we mentioned.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the categories 'an yaf‘ala' (to act) and 'an yanfa‘ila' (to be acted upon) do not undergo gradual change. The author begins by stating that some affirmed motion in them, but the truth is its falsity. The proof is given for 'an yaf‘ala' using the example of something moving from cold to hot. If the cold remained, both opposites would coexist; if it ceased, there would be a moment of rest, so no continuous transition. Then an objection is addressed: it is said that a thing gradually sheds its actuality. The author responds that this gradual shedding is not in the act itself but in the factors that complete the act: weakness of power, dissolution of resolve, or fatigue of the instrument, which change gradually. Thus, any supposed gradual change in 'an yaf‘ala' itself is actually in its accompaniments. Similarly for 'an yanfa‘ila', the change is in the recipient. The passage concludes by citing al-Mawaqif, affirming that these categories follow motion, but the motion is in the power or instrument or recipient.

Key Terms
أن يفعل
أن ينفعل
الفاعلية
القوة
الآلة
القابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think gradual change can occur in the category of action itself.
Correction: Gradual change does not occur in the act itself but in the accessories that complete the act, such as power, resolve, or instrument.

Checkpoint

A) Because both cold and heat would exist simultaneously.
B) Because if cold ceases, there is a time of rest before heat appears, so no continuum.
C) Because the subject does not receive gradual change.
D) Because motion is only in quantity and quality.

Key Takeaway
The categories of action and passion do not undergo gradual change; any perceived gradual change is actually in the power, instrument, or recipient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Non-Independence of Relational Attributes and Opposition
Answer to an objection affirming that relativity alone does not entail dependence of al-idafa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the non-independence of relational attributes (al-idafa) means they cannot directly receive opposition (tadadd).

Core Idea
Relational attributes follow their subjects and cannot independently receive opposites.

This passage addresses a subtle objection about whether simply being relative suffices to explain the non-independence of relations (al-idafa). The author clarifies that true non-independence means following the subject in rulings, not just being relative. Ibn Sina's argument about opposition further demonstrates this.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: an objection followed by a clarification.
The objector thinks the earlier explanation suffices; the author disagrees.
2
Distinguish relativity from dependence
Pay attention to the author's distinction: al-idafa's non-independence is not mere relativity, but following its subject in rulings. Why does this matter?
Otherwise categories like 'where' and 'position' would also be non-independent.
3
Examine Ibn Sina's argument
Ibn Sina shows that opposition can occur in other categories (where, when, position) but not in relations themselves, because relations are non-independent natures.
The hot-cold example illustrates that opposition in relations is derived from the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ما ذكر في الإضافة من عدم استقلالها كاف في الجميع على ما أشار إليه الإمام ولاحاجة إلى ما ذكروا من التطويل والتفصيل . قلنا : ليس معنى عدم استقلال الإضافة مجرد كونها نسبية، وإلا انتقض بالأين والوضع بل معناه كونها تابعة لمعروضها في الأحكام، ولهذا قال ابن سينا بعد إثبات التضاد في الأين ،والمتى ،والوضع، وأن يفعل، وأن ينفعل أن التضاد لا يعرض الإضافة، لأن الإضافات طبايع غير مستقلة بأنفسها، فيمتنع أن يعرض لها التضاد، لأن أقل درجات المعروض أن يكون مستقلاً بتلك المعروضية، وأما كون الأجر ضداً للأبرد ، كالحار للبارد فلأن الإضافة لما كانت طبيعة غير مستقلة، بل تابعة لمعروضها وجب أن يكون في هذا الحكم أيضاً ،تابعة وإلا لكانت مستقلة فيه .
If it is said: What was mentioned about al-idafa of its non-independence is sufficient for everything, according to what the Imam indicated, and there is no need for the lengthy detail they mentioned. We say: The meaning of the non-independence of al-idafa is not merely that it is relative, otherwise it would be invalidated by 'where' (al-ayn) and 'position' (al-wad‘). Rather its meaning is that it follows its subject in rulings. For this reason, Ibn Sina said after establishing opposition in 'where', 'when', 'position', 'to act', and 'to be acted upon', that opposition does not occur to al-idafa, because the al-idafat are non-independent natures by themselves, so it is impossible for opposition to occur to them, because the least degree of the recipient is that it be independent in that receptivity. As for something being hot as opposed to cold, like hot to cold, because al-idafa, since it is a non-independent nature, but rather follows its subject, must be in this ruling too, following, otherwise it would be independent in it.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that the earlier explanation of al-idafa's non-independence suffices for all similar cases, making further elaboration unnecessary. The answer clarifies that the non-independence of al-idafa is not simply its relativity—otherwise categories like 'where' (ayn) and 'position' (wad‘) would also be non-independent, which is not the case. Rather, its non-independence means it follows its subject in all rulings. Ibn Sina is cited as having established that opposition (tadadd) occurs in 'where', 'when', 'position', 'to act', and 'to be acted upon', but not in al-idafa itself. Why? Because al-idafat are non-independent natures, so they cannot be direct recipients of opposition, as the lowest degree of a recipient is independence in that property. However, when we say something is hot as opposed to cold, this is not a direct opposition in the relation but a consequence of the subject's opposition. Thus, al-idafa follows its subject even in this ruling, otherwise it would be independent.

Checkpoint

B) Because al-idafa is always instantaneously.
C) Because al-idafa is the same as 'where' and 'position'.
D) Because opposition only occurs in substances.

Key Takeaway
Al-idafa's non-independence means it follows its subject in rulings, not merely that it is relative; thus it cannot directly receive opposition.

Classification of Motion and Mover
Taftazani defines self-motion vs. accidental motion and divides mover into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between self-motion and accidental motion, and the three types of mover leading to forced, voluntary, and natural motion.

Core Idea
Motion is either essential or accidental; mover is either external or internal, with intention or without.

This passage from Taftazani clarifies the foundational categories of motion and mover in Islamic theology and physics. Before diving into deeper proofs, we need to agree on terms: what does it mean for something to be 'moving' in itself versus accidentally? And if something moves, what kind of force (mover) is behind it? Taftazani answers these questions in a concise classification that establishes the vocabulary for subsequent arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms in Arabic: متحرك بالذات, متحرك بالعرض, حركة قسرية, حركة إرادية, حركة طبيعية.
2
Identify the two main classifications
The passage first divides the moving thing (متحرك) into essential and accidental. Then it divides the mover (محرك) into three types based on externality and presence of intention.
Focus on the conditions: if mover is external -> forced; if internal with intention -> voluntary; if internal without intention -> natural.
3
Understand the fourfold division of absolute motion
Taftazani then clarifies that absolute motion has four types, but accidental motion always overlaps with one of the other three. Also note the broader definition of 'mover being in the moving thing' includes parts and special connections.
This avoids the misconception that accidental motion is a separate independent category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وأما المتحرك : فإن كانت الحركة فيه بالحقيقة فمتحرك بالذات كحركة السفينة وإلا فبالعرض كحركة راكبها . قال : وأما المحرك : فإن كان خارجاً عن ذات المتحرك، فالحركة قسرية، وإلا فإن كان قصد وشعور فإرادية، وإلا فطبيعية . يريد انقسام الحركة بالذات إلى الأقسام الثلاثة، وأما مطلق الحركة فينقسم إلى أربعة عرضية وقسرية وإرادية وطبيعية وإن كانت العرضية لا تخلو أو عن الأقسام الثلاثة، ولهذا قيل الحركة إن كانت تبعاً لحركة جسم آخر فعرضية، وإلا فإن كان محركها موجوداً في غير الجسم المتحرك فقسرية، وإن كان موجوداً فيه نفسه، فإن كان من شأنه الشعور والقصد فإرادية، وإلا فطبيعية، والمراد بكون المحرك في المتحرك أعم من أن يكون جزءاً منه أو به التعلق المخصوص كتعلق النفوس الإنسانية بأبدانها، والنفوس الفلكية بأفلاكها، فيعم تحرك الحجر هبوطاً والإنسان يمنة ويسرة، والفلك استدارة .
He said: As for the moving thing: if motion is in it in reality, then it is moving in itself (mutaḥarrik bi’l-dhāt) like the motion of a ship; otherwise, it is moving accidentally (bi’l-‘araḍ) like the motion of its rider. He said: As for the mover (muḥarrik): if it is external to the essence of the moving thing, then the motion is forced (qasriyya); otherwise, if there is intention and perception, then it is voluntary (irādiyya); otherwise, it is natural (ṭabī‘iyya). He intends the division of motion in itself into three divisions. As for absolute motion, it is divided into four: accidental, forced, voluntary, and natural, even though the accidental does not lack the other three divisions. Hence it is said: motion—if it is consequent upon the motion of another body, then it is accidental; otherwise, if its mover exists in a different thing from the moving body, then it is forced; if it exists in the body itself, then if it is characterized by perception and intention, it is voluntary; otherwise, it is natural. The intended meaning of the mover being in the moving thing is more general than it being a part of it or having a particular connection like the connection of human souls with their bodies and celestial souls with their spheres, so it includes the stone falling downward, man moving right and left, and the sphere rotating.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage is a classification of motion and mover. The author begins by distinguishing two types of moving things (mutaḥarrik): essential (bi’l-dhāt) and accidental (bi’l-‘araḍ). Essential motion is that which truly belongs to the object, like a ship moving by itself; accidental motion is attributed to an object by virtue of its association with another moving thing, like a rider moving because of the ship. Next, the mover (muḥarrik) is classified: if the mover is external to the moving object (like a hand pushing a stone), the motion is forced (qasriyya). If the mover is internal to the object, then if it involves perception and intention, the motion is voluntary (irādiyya)—e.g., animal movement. If internal but without perception and intention, it is natural (ṭabī‘iyya)—e.g., a stone falling. The author then clarifies that the above division applies to essential motion (ḥaraka bi’l-dhāt). For absolute motion, there are four types: accidental, forced, voluntary, and natural. Accidental motion always falls under one of the other three because it is secondary. Finally, the author expands on what it means for the mover to be 'in' the moving thing: it includes cases where the mover is a part of the object (like the stone's own nature) or has a particular connection, like the soul with the body or celestial souls with spheres. Thus, the classification covers the stone's natural fall, human voluntary movement, and the sphere's rotation.

Key Terms
متحرك بالذات
متحرك بالعرض
حركة قسرية
حركة إرادية
حركة طبيعية
حركة عرضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think accidental motion is a completely separate, independent fourth type of motion alongside forced, voluntary, and natural.
Correction: Taftazani clarifies that accidental motion is not an independent type; it always coincides with one of the other three because it is motion that depends on the motion of another body.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, which type of motion occurs when the mover is external to the moving object?
A) Voluntary
B) Natural
C) Forced
D) Accidental

Key Takeaway
Motion is classified by whether it is essential or accidental; the mover determines whether motion is forced, voluntary, or natural.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your daily actions—like moving your hand to pray or walking to the mosque—do you act from voluntary intention (حركة إرادية) or from habit (حركة طبيعية)? How often do you pause to ask yourself why you are moving, and whether your motion is really a conscious act of worship?
?
The passage distinguishes essential motion (as a ship) from accidental motion (as a rider). In your journey of faith, are you moving essentially—driven by your own sincere effort and reliance on Allah—or are you merely being carried by the crowd or by routine? What would it take for your spiritual motion to become essential?
?
Taftazani says natural motion comes from an internal mover without intention. Reflect on an area of your life where you act without thinking—like spending money or speaking. Is that motion natural to you, or have you allowed it to become natural by neglect? How can you transform that natural motion into a voluntary, intentional act of obedience to Allah?

Answering the Objection: All Motions Are Not Forced
Taftazani responds to the objection that on divine omnipotence all motions are forced.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani defends the classification of motion into forced, voluntary, and natural within an Ash'ari framework of divine omnipotence.

Core Idea
Customary causation preserves meaningful distinctions even under divine omnipotence.

The objection claims that if God creates all motions directly, then every motion is forced, making classification pointless. Taftazani responds by clarifying the meaning of 'mover' in such classifications, showing that Ash'ari occasionalism does not erase the distinction between voluntary and forced motions. This passage is a key example of how kalam reconciles divine power with human experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objection is framed as a question about the division of motion.
The objection challenges the very possibility of classifying motions.
2
Identify Taftazani's key move
Focus on the answer: 'Rather it is possible, because by 'mover' is intended that with which the creation of motion is customarily associated.' This is the core of the response.
He redefines the term 'mover' to mean the proximate customary cause, not the ultimate divine agent.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this preserves the division of motion into voluntary, forced, and natural. Even though God creates everything, the classification refers to customary associations with creatures' wills or external forces.
This shows that kalam uses ordinary language without undermining divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فعلى رأي من يجعل الممكنات كلها مستندة إلى الله تعالى ابتداء هل يتأتى هذا التقسيم أم تكون الحركات كلها قسرية؟ . قلنا : بل يتأتى بأن يراد بالمحرك ما جرت العادة بخلق الحركة معه كما يفصح عنه وصفهم بعض الحركات بكونه اختيارياً .
For it is said: According to the view of one who makes all contingent things dependent upon God Most High from the beginning, is this division possible, or are all motions forced? We say: Rather it is possible, because by 'mover' is intended that with which the creation of motion is customarily associated, as is made clear by their describing some motions as voluntary.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage presents and answers a theological objection. The objection states: if one holds that all contingent things (and thus all motions) are directly created by God (as Ash‘arites do), then the previous division of motion into forced, voluntary, and natural seems meaningless—since everything is ultimately caused by God, all motions would be 'forced' in a sense. Taftazani's answer is that the division remains valid because the term 'mover' in this classification refers not to the ultimate divine cause but to the customary proximate cause (mā jarat al-‘āda bi-khalq al-ḥaraka ma‘ahu). In other words, when we say a motion is voluntary, we mean that it is customarily associated with a creature's intention and will, even though God creates both the intention and the motion. The description of some motions as 'voluntary' (ikhtiyārī) by scholars confirms this usage.

Key Terms
ممكنات
قسرية
العادة
اختياري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'ari occasionalism makes all actions involuntary and indistinguishable.
Correction: Taftazani shows that voluntary action is defined by customary association with the creature's will, not by the absence of divine causation.

Checkpoint
How does Taftazani resolve the objection that on Ash‘ari occasionalism all motions would be forced?
A) He denies that all motions are from God
B) He redefines 'mover' as the customary cause
C) He limits the division to natural philosophers
D) He argues that forced motion does not exist

Key Takeaway
In Ash‘ari theology, divine causation does not negate voluntary action because voluntary motion is defined by its customary association with the creature's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that every choice you make is created by God, yet do you actually live as if your choices matter? How would your daily decisions change if you sincerely saw your will as the customary sign through which God creates your actions?
?
When you sin, do you secretly blame God's decree to ease your conscience, forgetting that your intention is the customary indicator of voluntary action? What does Taftazani's distinction demand from you when you stand before God accountable for your 'voluntary' sins?
?
You are not God's partner in causing actions, but your will is the marker of responsibility. Will you now examine your intentions more seriously, knowing that God creates them? What habit will you change tonight because of this truth?

The Dual Nature of Respiration: Voluntary and Natural
Taftazani explains that the motion of breathing is both voluntary and natural from different aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single motion like breathing can be classified as both voluntary and natural depending on the aspect considered.

Core Idea
A motion can be voluntary in its particulars but natural in its absolute necessity.

Taftazani here addresses a puzzle in classifying motion. While some motions are clearly voluntary or natural, breathing seems to share both characteristics. This passage resolves the apparent contradiction by showing that the same motion can be considered from two distinct perspectives: the absolute need for respiration makes it natural, while the ability to alter the timing of each breath makes it voluntary. We will explore how this insight applies to respiration and why it does not violate the general rules of motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice how Taftazani first states a general rule before addressing difficulties.
2
Identify the three types of motion
The passage mentions three motions: respiration, growth, and pulse. Note which are classified as natural and which as voluntary.
Growth and pulse are said to be natural, while respiration is both.
3
Understand the two aspects of respiration
Focus on the distinction between 'absolute need' (making it natural) and 'particular timing' (making it voluntary). This is the core idea.
The ability to hold your breath shows voluntary control over timing.
4
Consider the exception for simple bodies
Taftazani clarifies that the claim 'natural motion only goes one direction' applies only to simple elemental bodies, not to complex motions like respiration.
This removes a potential objection.
5
Appreciate the resolution
The passage concludes by reconciling the voluntary and natural aspects, citing Avicenna for support.
This is a model for handling apparent contradictions in classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: فحركة النفس إرادية : من حيث إمكان تغير جزئياتها عن أوقاتها وإن كانت طبيعية من حيث الاحتياج إلى مطلقها وحركة النمو طبيعية، وكذا النبض، ولا يبعد فيها اختلاف الجهات عند اختلاف الغايات وما قيل إن الطبيعية لا تكون إلا على نهج واحد، بل صاعدة أو هابطة، فذلك في البسائط العنصرية) . قد أشكل الأمر في بعض الحركات أنها من أي قسم من الأقسام الثلاثة لا سيما حركة النبض، فقد كثر اختلاف الناس في أنها طبيعية أو إرادية، وعلى التقديرين قابلية أو وضعية أو كمية ولكل من الفرق تمسكات مذكورة في المطولات سيما شروح الكليات ونحن نقتصر على ذكر ما هو أقرب وأصوب . فنقول: أما حركة النفس فإرادية باعتبار طبيعية باعتبار على ما قال بعض المتأخرين من الحكماء أنها تتعلق بالإرادة من حيث وقوع كل نفس في زمان يتمكن المتنفس من أن يقدمه على ذلك الزمان وأن يؤخره منه بحسب إرادته لكنها لا تتعلق بالإرادة من حيث الاحتياج الضروري إليها، فهو طبيعي من حيث الحاجة إلى مطلق التنفس، وإرادي من حيث إمكان تغير التنفسات الجزئية عن أوقات تقتضيها الحاجة، ويكون وقوعها في تلك الأوقات على مجراها الطبيعي، وهذا معى ما قال صاحب القانون أن حركة التنفس إرادية
He said: The motion of respiration (ḥaraka al-nafs) is voluntary (irādiyya) insofar as it is possible to change its particulars from their times, even though it is natural (ṭabī‘iyya) insofar as there is need for its absolute occurrence. The motion of growth is natural, and likewise the pulse. It is not far that it may have different directions according to different ends. And what is said: that natural motion is only in one course, either ascending or descending—that applies only to simple elemental bodies. The matter has been problematic regarding some motions, as to which of the three divisions they belong, especially the motion of the pulse. Many people have differed over whether it is natural or voluntary, and on both suppositions, whether it is qualitative, positional, or quantitative. Each group has proofs mentioned in lengthy works, especially commentaries on the Kulliyyāt. We confine ourselves to mentioning what is closer and more correct. We say: As for the motion of respiration, it is voluntary in one respect and natural in another, as some later philosophers have said: it is connected to the will insofar as each breath occurs at a time that the breather can advance or delay according to his will; but it is not connected to the will insofar as there is a necessary need for it. So it is natural in terms of the need for absolute respiration, and voluntary in terms of the possibility of changing the particular breaths from their times required by need, and their occurrence at those times is in their natural course. This is the meaning of what the author of the Canon said: that the motion of breathing is voluntary.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage discusses the classification of the motion of respiration (ḥaraka al-nafs/ التنفس) among the three types of motion (forced, voluntary, natural). The author first states a general rule: breathing is voluntary (irādiyya) because one can alter the timing of individual breaths, but it is also natural because absolute respiration is necessary for life. Similarly, growth and pulse are natural. He then notes a common claim that natural motion is always in one direction (e.g., stone downward, fire upward), but clarifies that this only applies to simple elemental bodies. The passage then acknowledges the difficulty of classifying certain motions, especially the pulse, and notes various opinions. However, the author chooses the most correct view: breathing is voluntary in one respect and natural in another. The reasoning is that every single breath can be voluntarily advanced or delayed, so it is voluntary in terms of particular timing; but there is an indispensable need for breathing in general, so it is natural in terms of absolute necessity. This combines both aspects without contradiction. The author cites Avicenna (the author of the Canon) in support.

Key Terms
حركة النفس
حركة النبض
حركة النمو
بسائط عنصرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A motion must be either entirely voluntary or entirely natural.
Correction: A single motion like breathing can be both voluntary in its particulars and natural in its absolute necessity, as Taftazani explains.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is caused by the soul
B) Because the breather can delay or advance the timing of each breath
C) Because it is not necessary for life
D) Because it is always ascending

Key Takeaway
The motion of respiration is voluntary in its particular timing but natural in its absolute necessity, reconciling two seemingly opposed classifications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the last breath you took. Did you consciously choose it, or did it happen without your thought? All day you shift between will and necessity. Where in your life are you pretending something is forced when it is actually chosen—or chosen when it is actually forced?
?
If even your breathing—so intimate and involuntary—is partly under your control, what does that say about the weight of your choices? You advance or delay each breath, but you cannot stop all breathing. Where else does your freedom meet its limit? And how will you use the freedom you do have today?
?
The author distinguishes between the absolute need for respiration and the timing of each breath. Is there a need in your life you have treated as absolute but actually allowed room for your will? Or a timing you have treated as up to you when it is actually required? What would it look like to live knowing that your very existence is a gift you neither earn nor fully control?

Objection and Answer on Voluntary Movement Under Divine Determinism
Objection and answer on classification of movements under divine determinism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the classification of voluntary and compulsory movements can be maintained even when all actions are ultimately from God.

Core Idea
"Voluntary" is a customary label, not a denial of divine causality.

The objection raised is a classic one: if everything is from God, how can any movement be called voluntary? The answer lies in understanding that the term 'mover' refers to the customary association between will and movement, not to the ultimate cause. This passage refines our understanding of how rational judgment categories apply within a deterministic framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the objection: if all contingent things are directly from God, then all movements would be compulsory, and the classification into voluntary and natural would fail.
Notice the phrase 'تكون الحركات كلها قسرية' which captures the core worry.
2
Identify the author's key move
The author redefines 'mover' (المحرك) as what is customarily associated with the creation of movement, not as the ultimate cause. This is the pivotal shift.
Look for the phrase 'ما جرت العادة بخلق الحركة معه'.
3
Reflect on how this preserves the classification
By basing the classification on customary association, the categories 'voluntary' and 'compulsory' remain meaningful as descriptive labels, even though all actions are from God.
Consider how this answer balances divine omnipotence with human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فعلى رأي من يجعل الممكنات كلها مستندة إلى الله تعالى ابتداء هل يتأتى هذا التقسيم أم تكون الحركات كلها قسرية؟ . قلنا : بل يتأتى بأن يراد بالمحرك ما جرت العادة بخلق الحركة معه كما يفصح عنه وصفهم بعض الحركات بكونه اختيارياً .
If it is said: According to the view of one who makes all contingent things dependent on God Almighty from the beginning, can this classification be made, or are all movements compulsory? We say: Rather it can be made by intending by 'mover' what is customarily associated with the creation of movement, as is clarified by their describing some movements as voluntary.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector raises a problem: if all contingent things are directly from God, then how can we classify movements into voluntary and natural? Wouldn't they all be compelled? The author replies that the classification is still possible if we understand 'mover' not as the ultimate cause but as what is customarily associated with the creation of movement. That is, we call a movement 'voluntary' when it is typically accompanied by will, even though the will itself is from God. Thus the classification is based on customary association, not on ultimate causal agency.

Key Takeaway
The classification of movements into voluntary and compulsory remains valid even under divine determinism because 'voluntary' refers to customary association with will, not to independent agency.
